Preface

partment of Health Technology, at the Technical University of Denmark, DTU, in partial
fulfilment of the degree Master of Science in Engineering in Physics and Nanotechnology,
MSc Eng and has an official workload of 35 ECTS.
It is assumed that the reader has a prior knowledge to electrostatics, calculus and the
field of semiconductor and MEMS manufacturing technologies.

Design, Fabrication and Characterization of a CMUT for a Linear Array Probe
29-07-2024
==PAGE:5==
Abstract
Ultrasound imaging is widely used in medical imaging as it is a non-invasive, fast, and
inexpensive imaging modality. However, it also has its limitations, specifically regarding
its resolution. The SURE project aims to develop ultrasound transducers and the tech-
nology to perform 3D super-resolution imaging of the erythrocytes in the bloodstream in
real-time, making high-resolution medical imaging more accessible for faster diagnosis of
cancer and vascular diseases. The SURE project is based on developing high frequency
Capacitative Micromachined Ultrasound Transducer (CMUT). For optimal imaging, these
transducers will have a resonance frequency of 15 MHz and a λ/2 pitch of 51.33 µm.
Choices regarding the designs and materials must be made, and the final dimensions
must be found through simulations to fulfil these requirements.
This master thesis’s purpose is to compare two methods of fabrication used previously
for fabricating 384-element linear arrays, namely a LOCal Oxidation of Silicon (LOCOS)
based process and an anodic bonding with a glass substrate process while attempting to
use a new metal for the adhesion layer of the bottom electrode. To compare these two
fabrication methods, this thesis will design, fabricate and characterise anodically bonded
CMUTs for a linear array probe using two different electrode configurations. To do this,
two previously fabricated CMUTs using the anodic bonding process are characterised to
determine the optimal design parameters. It was found that to limit charging effects, the
pull-in should not be more than 125 V, and the top electrodes should be etched using Ion
Beam Etching (IBE).
To design the CMUTs, Finite Element Method (FEM) simulations were used, two differ-
ent models were compared, and an optimal model was determined where the inclusion
of finding the resonance directly in a finalised linear array probe was added. Two ad-
ditional test arrays for experimentally determining Young’s modulus of the top plate and
the dependence of the pull-in voltage on the bottom electrode size were designed and
included in the lithography masks. The designed CMUT was then implemented in two
separate lithography mask sets where two different electrode configurations were used
to account for the difference in electrode configuration between the LOCOS and the an-
odically bonded CMUTs.
A wafer with anodically bonded CMUTs was successfully fabricated with a titanium (Ti)
adhesion layer for the bottom electrode and the same electrode configuration previously
used for the LOCOS process. Due to difficulties encountered during the fabrication, the
CMUTs with a Tantalum (Ta) adhesion layer for the bottom electrode and the electrode
configuration previously used for anodically bonded CMUTs were not finished during this
thesis.
The fabricated CMUTs was electrically characterised and showed a yield of 84.2 %, with
little to no charging effects and with an increase of the capacitance of 25 % during op-
eration. The stability was excellent, with a drop in the capacitance of only 1.0 % over
10 minutes. The resonance frequency was determined to be 20.04 MHz unbiased in air,
around 8 MHz lower than the designed value. Further characterisations are still needed
to understand the process-related issues, the electrical behaviour and further improve the
FEM models used to design the CMUTs. Still, this thesis has indicated it is possible to
fabricate CMUTs using an anodic bonding process, which has a larger increase in the ca-
pacitance than those made using a LOCOS based fabrication method. However, further
work is still needed to get the same yield as the LOCOS based fabrication method and to
compare the difference in electrode configuration.
Design, Fabrication and Characterization of a CMUT for a Linear Array Probe

1
Introduction
1.1
Medical Imaging - History and Differences
Humanity has always strived to go further than the previous generations. For going fur-
ther, one of the limiting factors is the technology necessary to go this extra distance, which
is constantly developing. The field of medical imaging is one area where significant strides
have been made over the last century. The first X-ray image taken by Wilhelm Röntgen in
1895 made it possible to see beneath the skin. [1] Since then, several improvements have
been made in X-ray imaging, obtaining a higher resolution while also going from 2D to 3D
images of patients with the inclusion of computed tomography (CT). Other imaging modal-
ities have also been invented in the form of Magnetic resonance imaging (MRI), Positron
Emission Tomography (PET), Single-Photon Emission Computed Tomography (SPECT),
where the addition of CT also has made it possible to perform 3D imaging on patients. [2]
These characteristics mean the imaging modalities are excellent at detecting tumours or
other foreign objects. [2] However, these imaging modalities also have downsides: X-ray
imaging relies on sending ionising X-rays through a patient. The amount that an average
person is exposed to X-rays will be negligible. However, reducing the amount as much
as possible is still for the best. MRI can heat the tissue during longer scans and can also
be incompatible with implants as they often contain metal, and MRI scanner uses strong
magnetic fields, which can turn ordinary objects into projectiles. [3] PET and SPECT
also only work when injecting a contrast fluid into the patient, which can have adverse
side effects for the patient. They are also all reliant on large, stationary and expensive
equipment. Consequently, the facilities and the budgets for hospitals and health clinics
need to accommodate these imaging modalities, which may not always be the case. The
imaging modalities may also not be sufficiently available. Limiting pre-emptive screenings
for diseases such as cancer. Despite this, they are the imaging modalities with the best
resolution.
As an alternative to these imaging modalities, there is ultrasound, which uses much
smaller and more mobile transducers fitting into the palm of the hand and is cheaper
than the other imaging modalities. It is also generally regarded as one of the safest imag-
ing modalities. [4] However, it lacks both the resolution and 3D capabilities compared to
the other imaging modalities. It also has limits as it relies on emitting, called the pulse,
and detecting the reflected ultrasound wave, called the echo. The depth at which it is
possible to image the body is limited as the pulse needs to reach the given depth, and the
echo needs to have enough energy to be returned to the ultrasound transducer. Since
the reflections depend on changes based on the difference in density of the tissue, both
low- and high-density tissue can be hard to image, and a large change in density means
the reflected signal will be high. A high reflectance also means that only a small part of
the signal will propagate further into the body. Thus, only a small part of the body will be
imaged. Two body parts that can cause issues are the lungs, which have low density, and
the bones, which have high density; therefore, they are two of the most difficult parts to
image with ultrasound. [2]
1.1.1
Ultrasound - How Does It Work?
An ultrasound transducer’s transduction principle is that it can convert electrical energy
to mechanical energy, and then acoustic energy, from acoustic energy, and back to elec-
trical energy. An electrical bias is applied to the transducer, causing the elements in
the transducer to vibrate. This vibration converts the electrical energy from the bias into
Design, Fabrication and Characterization of a CMUT for a Linear Array Probe
1
==PAGE:10==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
mechanical vibration, which causes the pulse to be emitted from the transducer into the
medium in front of it. To detect the returned echo. The electrical bias is no longer applied.
Instead, the incoming echo will induce vibrations in the transducer. Thus, the incoming
acoustic energy is converted into mechanical energy and then converted into an electrical
signal. When combined with the time between the emitted pulse and when the returned
echo is received, the electrical signal can then be used to create a grey-scale image of the
intensity of the acoustic wave, which will be the final ultrasound image. These two modes
of operation are called the transmit and receive modes. Since the same transducer both
emits and receives the ultrasound signal used to generate the final image. The resolution
of the transducer is crucial as it is the limiting factor. The resolution can be divided into
three parts: The spatial, temporal and contrast resolution.
The transducer determines the spatial resolution. The temporal resolution is defined by
the time it takes to obtain a frame, and the contrast resolution is from the contrast differ-
ence between two different and adjacent objects. [5] For the spatial resolution, it consists
of an axial and a lateral component. The axial resolution is how well objects parallel to
the ultrasound propagation can be resolved. Increasing the frequency will decrease the
wavelength and give a better axial resolution but increase the attenuation of the acoustic
wave, giving a decrease in the penetration depth. [5] The lateral resolution depends on
the beam’s width, meaning that a more focused beam will have a higher spatial resolution.
Thus, acoustic lenses are needed for ultrasound transducers. [5] The contrast resolution
is the transducer’s sensitivity to the incoming acoustic wave, so designing more sensitive
transducers will help differentiate different tissues, thus increasing the resolution of ultra-
sound imaging. The transducer’s resolution also needs to be improved, as increasing the
number of elements will increase the resolution. [6]
1.2
State of Ultrasound and the SURE Project
This thesis was carried out in connection with the SURE project at the MEMS applied
sensors group at DTU Health Tech. The SURE project aims to develop transducers and
methods for obtaining Super Resolution Ultrasound Real Time Imaging of Erythrocytes
(SURE).[7] In practice, this means that the overall SURE project aims to develop ultra-
sound transducers, ultrasound scanners and the remaining technology to be able to pro-
duce 3D ultrasound images in real-time with a resolution high enough to be able to see
and track erythrocytes, red blood cells. Achieving this goal requires a resolution of 50
microns, which is a 100-400 fold improvement in resolution compared to state-of-the-art
3D ultrasound imaging. Such a high resolution will open up the way for faster detection
of diseases such as cancer, diabetes, and vascular diseases, generally all the way down
to the capillary level. [7]
Currently, high-resolution ultrasound relies on contrast agents to increase resolution. [8]
The downside is that an injection of fluid is needed, and the lifetime in the body is limited.
[8] The goal of the SURE project is to develop the necessary technology to increase the
resolution without the need for contrast agents. [7] This will improve the conditions for the
patient while also decreasing the time needed to perform a scan up to 2600 times. [7]
For achieving these objectives, a 3D ultrasound image will be needed, and to achieve a
3D image, a 2D transducer design is required since the extra dimension of the transducer
will give the necessary information to construct a 3D image. Currently, most ultrasound
transducers on the market are 1D arrays where there are N linear elements. Thus, they
are only capable of creating 2D images. All elements are oriented in one direction as
shown in the top of fig. 1.1. Typical element counts for commercial transducers are in the
range of 128-256 elements. For a 2D array, two 1D elements are placed orthogonally,
2
Design, Fabrication and Characterization of a CMUT for a Linear Array Probe
==PAGE:11==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
Figure 1.1: At the top, a linear array is shown. Each vertical line is an element, and
one of the electrodes is separated from neighbouring elements. Below is a row-column
addressed array showing how the sub-elements are defined. Figure as reproduced in [9],
and original figure from [6].
where the intersections between the two elements are called sub-elements. An example
is shown at the bottom of fig. 1.1.
For a 2D array, the N by N grid of elements means the total number of sub-elements
will be N2. When such a transducer needs to be connected to a scanner, a total of N2
connections will also be required. A 1D array typically has 128 elements, and for a 2D
transducer, a total of 1282 = 16384 connections need to be made to address each sub-
element individually. This is a staggeringly high number of connections, and to achieve
the desired resolution in the SURE project, transducers with element counts in the order of
1024x1024 elements are needed. Therefore, a Row-Column addressed array is typically
used instead, which will only require 2N connections and will scale better for larger arrays.
Only the row or the column needs to be connected to the scanner. The difference between
linear and row-column addressed arrays can be seen in fig. 1.1.
For this thesis, the focus will be limited to 1D arrays, also called linear arrays. These
will be used as a technology-developing platform to develop the necessary technology
and simulation models for designing the 2D CMUT arrays. This choice was made as the
1D arrays are simpler to fabricate, and it will be easier to find defects during electrical
characterisations as they are only located along the single bad element and not across a
row and a column. The developed process for fabrication and models used for designing
transducers can then be transferred for fabricating 2D row-column addressed arrays.
1.3
CMUTs in Ultrasound Imaging
Traditionally, ultrasound transducers have been made using piezoelectric materials. These
materials contract or expand when an electric bias is applied to them. Likewise, when
external forces mechanically deform them, they generate electric potential. Therefore,
Design, Fabrication and Characterization of a CMUT for a Linear Array Probe
3
==PAGE:12==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
piezoelectric materials have been on the market for many years and are a well-established
technology as they fulfil the criteria needed for an ultrasound transducer. They are also
well established in other industries within sensors and actuators used in anything from
AFMs to inkjet printers. [10, 11] The most widely used piezoelectric ceramic is lead zir-
conium titanate (PZT). This material can be grown in various shapes and sizes and has
a strong piezoelectric effect. Yet, it is problematic in terms of its toxicity. As the name im-
plies, it contains lead, and the ceramic is known for its toxicity, environmental hazard, and
carcinogenic hazard. [12] For these reasons, the medical industry seeks to phase out the
use of PZT in the production of ultrasound transducers and replace them with lead-free
alternatives; it should be noted that the lead in the transducer is encapsulated and never
exposed to the patient. Such an alternative is CMUTs. These can be fabricated using
micro- and nanofabrication technology to achieve smaller elements. Thus, it is possible
to fit more elements on the same surface area than transducers made using PZTs. While
also achieving more accurate and uniform dimensions. All of these are key elements in
achieving high-resolution ultrasound imaging.
The CMUT technology is still relatively new and emerged in the 1990s as an alternative
to PZT transducers, with the first publication on it in 1994 by Matthew I. Haller and Butrus
T. Khuri-Yakub. [13] The principle of transduction of a CMUT is similar to that of a PZT
transducer. It also converts the electrical energy into an acoustic wave through a mechan-
ical oscillation. However, the way it is converted is different. In a CMUT, the mechanical
oscillation is no longer a vibrating crystal. Instead, a CMUT is formed by a cavity over
which a thin membrane is suspended and with a thin electrode on top of it. Inside the
cavity, a fixed electrode is placed. This geometry forms a small capacitor called a CMUT
cell. A sketch of one can be seen in fig. 1.2. The membrane typically has a height of a few
microns, around one-tenth of a hair’s width, so it can vibrate when an alternating electrical
bias is applied. These vibrations will create acoustic pressure waves, which can then be
used to transmit an ultrasound pulse. The alternating bias will drive the operation of the
CMUT, making it possible to control when an acoustic wave is emitted. An incoming echo
can also vibrate the top plate. These vibrations will change the average distance between
the two plates, changing the capacitance of a CMUT cell, which can be detected electri-
cally and thus convert the incoming acoustic energy into electrical energy and a signal to
produce an ultrasound image.
When operating a CMUT, The two different modes are a bit different than those presented
earlier. Those were for a PZT transducer. For a CMUT transducer, both a DC and an
AC bias are used during the operation. In the receive mode, RX, a DC bias is applied
to the cell so that the charge accumulates, making the cell operate as a capacitor. The
average distance between the two electrodes changes when an echo reaches the CMUT.
Changing the capacitance of the CMUT by measuring this change in capacitance and
sending it to an ultrasound scanner, the change in capacitance can be converted into an
ultrasound image. In the transmit mode, TX, both the DC bias and an AC bias are applied.
The AC bias will make the top plate oscillate, thus creating the pulse from the acoustic
pressure wave. The DC bias is still applied in the transmit mode as it will pre-tension the
top plate, making it easier for the AC signal to vibrate the top plate. The circuit diagrams
for the two modes are also shown in fig. 1.2.
For CMUTs, a cell’s dimensions and shape can vary greatly depending on the final appli-
cation. Two of the most common shapes are circles and squares, which have proven to
work. [14, 9] In this thesis, the focus will be on circular cells. Likewise, there are several
different ways to fabricate the CMUTs. The two methods currently used in the MEMS ap-
plied sensors group are a glass substrate and anodic bonding to create the top plate. The
4
Design, Fabrication and Characterization of a CMUT for a Linear Array Probe
==PAGE:13==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
other is based on selectively oxidising a silicon substrate and then, through fusion bond-
ing, creating the top plate. Each method has its advantages and disadvantages. The most
crucial thing is that the process using a silicon substrate can take longer to fabricate and
is more particle-sensitive. The glass substrate process can be faster, cheaper, and less
sensitive to particles. However, all of them share similar characteristic dimensions which
define the electrical and acoustical function of the CMUT. These are the radius/length of
the cell, a, the gap height, g, the top plate thickness, h, electrode width/radius, w. How
they are defined is also sketched in fig. 1.2. However, the figure is not drawn to scale,
as the dimensions can range from several micrometres all the way down to a hundred
nanometres. It is only an artistic drawing that gives an overview of the overall structure.
Figure 1.2: Sketch of two CMUT cells not drawn to scale. both cells simplify the full
geometry of a CMUT cell where the bottom electrode is placed inside the cavity. On the
left, a cell is drawn with the cell radius, a, gap height, g, electrode width/radius, w, and
the top plate thickness, h, shown. The height here is shown as the height without the top
electrode thickness. On the right, a CMUT cell is shown with the electrical connections
for its operation. In transmit mode, TX, both a DC bias and an AC bias are needed
to emit an ultrasonic pulse where the DC bias will pull the top plate down towards the
bottom electrode for a spring-softening effect, and the AC bias will then make the top
plate oscillate to emit the ultrasonic pulse. For the receive mode, RX, The same DC bias
is still needed, but an incoming ultrasound echo will make the top plate oscillate, which
can be detected as a change in the capacitance of the CMUT.
1.4
Scope of Thesis
This thesis will focus on designing, fabricating and characterising 384 linear element
CMUT arrays for a linear array probe using a glass substrate. These have previously
been successfully fabricated with a silicon substrate. [15, 14] The goal is to design and
fabricate an array which is as similar as possible to these silicon-based arrays but with
a glass substrate instead to be able to compare the two methods of fabrication and de-
termine the difference between the final function of the two fabrication processes. Two
designs will be made to ensure they are as comparable as possible and to account for the
difference in the electrode layout in the previously fabricated arrays. The silicon-based
array had a common bottom electrode. [15] While the glass-based arrays normally have
a common top electrode. [9]. Therefore, two designs will be made, one with a common
top electrode and one with a common bottom electrode. A glass-based common top elec-
trode linear CMUT array has been fabricated earlier but suffered from several electrical
defects from the fabrication. [16, 17, 18] Since then, significant process optimisation has
been performed and will be included in this work. [9, 17] A common bottom electrode

1.5
Thesis Layout
This thesis has been divided into several chapters. Each chapter is dedicated to the
different parts of the work carried out during the thesis. However, as each chapter builds
on the chapters before them, it is recommended to read the chapters in succession for a
full overview of the work carried out.
Chapter 1 - Introduction: This chapter gives an introduction to a selected number of
medical imaging modalities, The relevance of CMUTs, how CMUTs can be used in ultra-
sound transducers and the principle of transduction for CMUTs.
Chapter 2 - Theory: This chapter describes the theory behind the electrical character-
istics and operation of CMUTs such as the capacitance and the resonance frequency.
Lastly, it also covers selected topics of micro- and nanofabrication.
Chapter 3 - Electrical Measurement Setups: This chapter gives an overview of the
method and setups used for performing electrical characterisation on the fabricated CMUT
arrays.
Chapter 4 - Initial Characterisation: This chapter describes the characterisation per-
formed on two previously fabricated wafers and what was learned regarding process op-
timisation for the newly designed CMUT arrays.
Chapter 5 - Design: This chapter outlines the requirements for the new CMUT arrays.
How FEM simulations were used to find the desired dimensions and parameters for the
CMUT arrays, along with the improvements made to the simulation models used.
Chapter 6 - Wafer Map: This chapter gives an overview of the two sets of lithography
masks made in this thesis, their differences and similarities. Along with the different arrays
present in the design and their naming convention.
Chapter 7 - Fabrication: This chapter describes the fabrication of the wafers with the
CMUT arrays, along with the difficulties in processing and how they were solved.
Chapter 8 - Electrical Characterisation: This chapter gives an overview of the electrical
characterisation performed on the wafer, which was successfully fabricated and the results
of the characterisation.
Chapter 9 - Discussion: This chapter will go into detail with some of the process-related
issues encountered, along with possible reasons and suggestions for further work. The
results of the electrical characterisation will also be discussed, as well as their meaning
and the further characterisations needed.
Chapter 10 - Conclusion & Outlook: This chapter will give a final overview of the work
done during this thesis, the conclusions which could be reached and an poverview of the
work which remains to be done.

2
Theory
This chapter aims to familiarise the reader with the theory needed to understand the oper-
ating principles behind CMUTs, expanding on the concepts explained in the introduction
in chapter 1. Both in terms of the concepts and operating principles, as well as the mathe-
matical foundation and analytical expressions needed for designing a CMUT array. Also,
the theory behind some of the crucial process steps within the fabrication of CMUTs will
be presented.
2.1
General Theory of CMUTs
Figure 2.1: Sketch of a parallel plate ca-
pacitor. The capacitance is determined by
the surface area of the electrode, A, the
gap height between the electrodes, g, and
the permittivity of the material between the
electrodes, ε, as shown in eq. (2.1).
The previous chapter mentioned that a CMUT
is a microfabricated structure; more specif-
ically, it belongs to the category of devices
called Micro Electro Mechanical Systems
(MEMS). It was also mentioned that the DC
bias is always applied to the cell to deter-
mine capacitance. It also has a secondary
purpose: the DC bias will slightly bend down
the top plate. Pretensioning the top plate and
giving a spring-softening effect. [19] This low-
ers the effective spring constant of the top
plate, ensuring that the same applied AC bias
will make the top plate vibrate more. Thus, a
stronger pulse is emitted. At the same time,
for detecting the incoming echo. The spring
softening effect will make the top plate more sensitive and vibrate more, making it easier
to detect the incoming echo, and a stronger electrical signal will be formed from the echo.
[19] Thus, the spring softening effect is crucial for the operation of the device.
In the introduction, the structure of the CMUT was introduced in fig. 1.2. What was not
introduced was how each parameter influences the final operation of a CMUT, especially
the resonance frequency and the pull-in voltage. Like any other mechanical structure,
a CMUTs top plate will have its own natural resonance frequency where the CMUT will
emit the pulse and detect the incoming echo more efficiently. Therefore, the resonance
frequency is an essential parameter to know as it will determine the frequency at which
the transducer will operate. Another critical parameter is the pull-in voltage, the maximal
bias that can be applied to the cell. Both of these parameters are linked to the geometrical
dimensions of the cell, and for designing a CMUT as described in chapter 5, their influence
on both parameters needs to be understood.
2.2
Electrical Characteristics of CMUTs
The electrical behaviour and operation of a CMUT array can be explained from a sin-
gle CMUT cell. An array is, after all, a collection of elements, where each element is
several CMUT cells in a parallel connection. In this section, the reader is expected to
understand electrostatics, calculus and mathematics similar to the one taught in the engi-
neering physics program at DTU. Thus, this section is a more theory-heavy section. The
focus will also be on circular CMUT cells, as these were considered in this thesis. Other
geometries also exist. One example is square cells, which have also been worked on
extensively in the MEMS applied sensors group. [9]

2.2.1
Capacitance of a CMUT cell
A CMUT cell consists of two electrodes: the bottom electrode and the top electrode. The
simplest model to obtain the capacitance of the CMUT cell is the parallel plate capacitor
where two plates of equal surface area, A, are separated by a fixed distance, which for
CMUTs is called the gap height, g, with a medium with a permittivity, ε, in between them
and is stated in eq. (2.1). The situation is shown in fig. 2.1.
C = ϵA
g .
(2.1)
However, this model assumes that the bottom electrode and top plate are fixed and will
not move when a voltage bias is applied across the two electrodes. In the general case
of capacitors, this is a valid assumption. For a CMUT, this is not the case. The operation
principle of CMUTs is that the applied AC bias will make the top plate oscillate, thus send-
ing out the ultrasound pulse. Likewise, the detection of the echo works by the incoming
echo deflecting the top plate. So, for describing the capacitance of the CMUT cell, the de-
flection of the top plate must be included. This deflection of the top plate can be described
by letting the gap height depend on the spatial coordinates. For a circular geometry, the
rotation symmetry can be exploited, and thus, the deflection will only depend on the radial
coordinate, r, measured from the centre of the cell. This simplification leads to a new
model for the capacitance on the form
Ccell =

the case of the parallel plate capacitor as stated earlier in eq. (2.1). The surface area of
the electrodes can be calculated using a surface integral of the two spatial coordinates,
the radius and the polar angle, leading to the following double-integral

2.2.2
Pull-in voltage
As mentioned, an electric field must be applied to operate the CMUTs. However, if the
attractive force from the electric bias between the top plate and the bottom electrode
becomes too great, a point will come where the electrostatic forces will be larger than the
spring forces pulling the top plate up. After this point, the top plate will be pulled down to
the bottom electrode and collapse the CMUT cell. This phenomenon, called pull-in, can
potentially short-circuit the CMUT cell. In pull-in, the top plate will also be limited in its
vibration due to its contact with the bottom electrode. Therefore, pull-in is undesired. So,
knowing what magnitude of the applied bias will make the cell go into pull-in is essential
when designing CMUTs. This voltage is called the pull-in voltage, Vpi. Thus, when the
pull-in voltage is applied, the top plate is in the last stable position. In order to determine
the pull-in voltage. The CMUT cell can be considered as a closed system, and the energy
to be conserved; this way, the pull-in voltage can be found. The total energy in the system
will be the sum of the electrical energy from the applied bias, the spring energy from the
plate bending, and the energy from the pressure difference across the membrane. Giving
the following energy consideration
Utotal = Uplate −Uelectric −Upressure = 1
2k0w2
0 −pw0A0 −1
2V 2C(wo).
(2.7)
The electric energy is subtracted as it is an external force that works on the system. The
case is the same for the pressure difference. k0 is the spring constant of the plate, p is the
external pressure, and A0 is the total area of the electrodes. For this, only the work done
by static forces is considered since the position to be found is the last stable position.
For example, it is irrelevant if the applied bias is AC or DC. The magnitude of the bias is
the relevant parameter. [21] Taking the derivative with respect to the centre deflection in
eq. (2.7). The total force on the top plate is found

2.2.3
Resonance Frequency
The resonance frequency of the top plate of a CMUT can be calculated by seeing it as a
circular clamped plate. The resonance of clamped plates is a problem that has already
been investigated in mechanical engineering. In the derivation of the resonance of the top
plate, it is assumed that it only consists of a single layer. Later, the additional layers are
included through FEM simulations. Like the pull-in voltage, the top-plate thickness is also
found through energy considerations. The method used for this work is the Rayleigh-Ritz
method developed by Lamb in 1920 to give the ratio of the resonance frequency of the
plate in immersion with the resonance frequency in vacuum [22]
ωr
ω0
=
1
√1 + β =
1
q
1 + Γ a
h
ρm
ρp
(2.12)
The frequency ratio of the resonance frequencies is ωr/ω0, and ρm and ρp are the density
of the medium and plate. The factor γ is the non-dimensionalized added virtual mass
incremental factor (NAVMI). It is included as the medium right in front of the plate can
be modelled as an added mass for the top plate. Thus, the medium will dampen the
oscillation of the top plate. It will be dependent on the geometry and how the plate is
clamped to the substrate. It can also be seen from eq. (2.12) that the aspect ratio affects
the resonance frequency ratio. This model is valid for thin plates, which means for cases
where h/a ≤0.1. [22] For a top plate with a height of 2 µm. The radius would have to be
at least 20 µm. For the NAVMI factor, the value calculated by Lamb for circular plates is
Γ = 0.6689. [22] For CMUTs the material used for the top plate is typically poly crystalline
Silicon (poly-silicon) which has a density of around ρp = 2.330 g/cm−3, and the medium is
water which has the density ρm,water = 1.000 g/cm−3. However, ultrasound transducers
are not placed directly in contact with the skin. An insulating layer and matching layers
are placed on top of the transducer. Often Polydimethylsiloxane (PDMS) is used where
ρm,PDMS = 1.37 g/cm−3. [23]
The equation in eq. (2.12) by Lamb only gives the ratio. The resonance frequency in
a vacuum is needed to find the resonance in water or any other medium. Luckily, the
resonance frequency of clamped plates is well described within mechanical engineering.

From this, the radius and top-plate thickness can be found. In practice, the above equation
would have the radius replaced with an effective radius, aeff, along with converting from
the angular frequency ωr to the centre-frequency f = 2πωr. The above equation is also
a simplification of the real resonance as it only considers the case of a single material
plate. In real CMUTs, the top plate will consist of several materials. Each will have its own
Young’s modulus, and stresses will also be present due to the different lattice constants
and compositions of the materials. Therefore, eq. (2.14) can be used to see how the
various parameters affect the resonance frequency. For example, it can still be used that
increasing the radius will decrease the resonance frequency. Still, the exact resonance
frequency will have to be found from FEM simulations to consider all the different aspects
of a multilayered top plate.
2.2.4
Charging in CMUTs
One of the main issues when operating CMUTs comes from the charging of the insulating
dielectric layers between the two electrodes. Since CMUTs have a low electromechani-
cal coupling coefficient, the DC bias used to operate them is generally high and can be
several hundred volts. [25] This high applied bias can lead to charges being stored or
trapped in the CMUT cells during the operation. These are unwanted as they can lead to
a higher chance of dielectric breakdowns and changes to the electrical and mechanical
properties of the CMUT. [25] For example, the capacitance of a CMUT can change over
time when electrical charges are built up. [25] This will change the performance of the
CMUTs over time and could also give different measurements from day to day, which is
highly undesirable. To detect if a given CMUT suffers from charging, one of the main
methods used is Capacitance-Voltage (CV) measurements where the zero point for the
CV curve will shift over time. [25]
2.3
Electrical Characterisation
A CMUT cell can be characterised electrically with an equivalent circuit like any other
electrical component, see fig. 2.2. In the case of a single CMUT cell, the equivalent circuit
consists of two capacitors and two resistors. The first capacitor, Ccmut, is the CMUT
itself. The second capacitor Cp is the parasitic capacitance from neighbouring circuit
components, which has an unwanted but unavoidable effect on circuits. The resistance
comes from the connection to the CMUT cell itself, Rs, and from the leak current over
a capacitor, RL. The leakage current will be low due to the insulating dielectric layer
separating the bottom and top electrodes. Thus, the leak current and resistance can be
used to verify if there are short circuits in a CMUT.
Design, Fabrication and Characterization of a CMUT for a Linear Array Probe
11
==PAGE:20==
CHAPTER 2. THEORY
Figure 2.2: A sketch of the
equivalent circuit for a sin-
gle CMUT cell.
Since CMUTs are also driven with an AC signal, the electrical
impedance of the circuit also needs to be understood. The
AC signal means there will be an electrical impedance in the
circuit to oppose the movement of the current. Just like the
resistance of a circuit does in a DC circuit. However, the
impedance will have a real and an imaginary part. The real
part is the magnitude of the impedance, and the imaginary
part is the phase of the electrical impedance. The impedance
can then be written on the form
ZE = |ZE| · ei·arg(ZE) = R + iX.
(2.15)
Here, the impedance’s magnitude is the circuit’s resistance,
R, and the phase is the reactance and is denoted by X. By
using the coordinates of the imaginary plane, resistance and reactance can be rewritten
using the phase angle, θ:
R = |ZE| · cos θ
(2.16)
X = |ZE| · sin θ
(2.17)
The magnitude of the impedance is then given as
|ZE| =
p
R2 + X2
(2.18)
Thus, the magnitude of the electrical impedance can be calculated from the circuit’s resis-
tance and reactance. The phase angle θ is the phase difference between the current I and
the voltage V for the circuit. Thus, when describing the voltage and current as sinusoidal
functions, they can be defined as:
V = V0sin(ωt)
(2.19)
I = I0sin(ωt + θ)
(2.20)
From these equations, the phase angle, θ, can be determined from measurements of the
current and voltage when sweeping the AC current frequency.
2.3.1
Impedance of Individual Electrical Components
Each component type in fig. 2.2 will have different impedances and phase angles. The
phase angle will depend on whether the current leads the voltage or the voltage leads
the current. In a capacitor, the charge stored is directly proportional to the voltage over it.
Hence, the current must lead over the voltage to build up the charge for this to happen.
Thus, the phase difference between the voltage and current is -90°. The opposite is the
case for an inductor. Here, the current builds up slower than the voltage. It thus has a
phase angle of 90°. In the case of a resistor, neither the current nor the voltage leads
the other. Hence, the phase angle of a resistor will be 0°. In terms of impedances, the
impedance of the individual components are:
ZC =
1
iωC
(2.21)

CHAPTER 2. THEORY
ZL = iωL
(2.22)
ZR = R
(2.23)
In this expression, the capacitance is C, the inductance is L, the resistance is R, and the
frequency is ω. Converting the magnitude of the impedance to a logarithmic scale for
each of the components it is obtained:
log(|ZC|) = log

(2.24)

For a capacitor, the plot for the magnitude of the impedance of the circuit will be propor-
tional to a declining frequency, in other words. For a fixed capacitance, the magnitude of
the impedance will drop linearly with the frequency when plotted in a double logarithmic
plot. For an inductor, when the inductance is fixed, the magnitude of the impedance will
increase linearly with the frequency. For a resistor, the logarithm of the impedance was
not calculated as it would not be frequency-dependent, as seen in eq. (2.23). Instead,
it will be constant with the frequency. These characteristics are helpful as they allow for
quickly examining impedance plots and determining how the Device Under Test (DUT).
For CMUTs, it can quickly be seen if they suffer from short circuits or other defects by
examining the magnitude of the impedance and the phase angle. The capacitance can
also be found using the same measurement as the by using eq. (2.21) by fitting the ca-
pacitance to the impedance as a function of the frequency. For an element of CMUT cells,
the measured capacitance will be that of all of the cells in a parallel connection, including
the parasitic capacitances. This means the measured capacitance of an element will be
given by:
Celement = CCMUT,1 + Cp,1 + CCMUT,2 + Cp,2 + ... + CCMUT,N + Cp,N
(2.26)
Where N denotes the total number of CMUT cells in one element. This capacitance will
then be a combination of the capacitance of the individual cells and the parasitic capaci-
tance.
2.4
FEM Simulations
FEM simulations are an essential tool within physics and material science as it allows
for solving complex problems in situations where an analytical solution will be difficult or
impossible to obtain. It is often used within electromagnetism, fluid dynamics, structural
analysis and, generally, any engineering problem where partial differential equations ap-
pear for this exact reason. One example is which dimensions should a CMUT have for
it to have a specific resonance frequency and pull-in voltage? The analytical models de-
scribed earlier can be used but are tedious and challenging to solve for multi-material top
plates. But by using FEM simulations, it is possible. FEM simulations work by dividing the
geometry and structure of the system to be solved into smaller sub-elements of finite size,
thus the name finite element. These elements constitute the mesh of the system where
the size of the elements can vary greatly, and their size is chosen when setting up the
FEM simulations. A set of equations, called element equations, can then be constructed
within each of the finite elements. These are simplifications of the original governing par-
tial differential equations, and the initial values can be used to find numerical solutions
to the element equations and, by that extension, solve the overall problem numerically.
Design, Fabrication and Characterization of a CMUT for a Linear Array Probe
13
==PAGE:22==
CHAPTER 2. THEORY
A finer mesh (smaller size of the elements) will give more accurate results. Still, at the
same time, it also increases the computational time needed to solve the model, as more
equations will have to be solved. However, at a certain element size, the increased ac-
curacy of making the mesh smaller will diminish, as when going to a higher order in a
Taylor polynomial. So, the element size needs to constantly be optimised to balance the
accuracy compared to the computational time, and a trade-off typically has to be made.
Thus, FEM provides an alternative way of solving many design problems where the initial
values of the system are known, for example, in the case of CMUTs. FEM simulations can
be used to determine the desired radius, top-plate height, gap height and pull-in voltage
to obtain a desired resonance frequency from initial estimates of the dimensions of the
CMUTs.
2.5
Fabrication Theory
This section will cover the theory for some of the methods used in the fabrication process,
explain their mechanisms, advantages and disadvantages, and discuss typical fabrication
problems that can be encountered.
2.5.1
Bonding Methods
Different methods have been developed for the bonding of wafers to each other depend-
ing on the materials of the wafers to be bonded. Generally, wafer bonding has been used
extensively with the field of MEMS, where one of the first use cases was fabricating pres-
sure sensors. [26] In this section, the theory of wafer bonding will be limited to anodic
bonding and fusion bonding as these two methods are the only two used currently by the
MEMS applied sensors group. Several other methods exist for different applications and
stacks of materials. An excellent overview of various other methods is provided by K.
Najafi et al. in Comprehensive Microsystems[26].
Anodic Bonding
Anodic bonding is a wafer bonding method used to bond a glass wafer to a silicon wafer in
a permanent bond. [26] The bond strength has been shown to be stronger than both the
glass and silicon itself, leading to the bonded stack cracking instead of the bond separating
when trying to pull the wafers apart.[27] To bond the wafers to each other, they must first
be placed in contact where a temporary bond is formed initially by van der Waal adhesion
forces. At this point, the wafers can still be separated from each other again. The bond
between the two wafers is only made final when heated to 300-450 °C, and an electrical
bias is subsequently applied across the wafer stack. The heating will make the surface
of the wafers more malleable and increase the mobility of atoms inside the wafers. The
electrical bias must be applied correctly by connecting the glass wafer to the cathode and
the silicon wafer to the anode. This electrical bias will then draw positive charges towards
the cathode; typically, this will be Na+ ions, creating a depletion layer at the interface. The
situation is shown in fig. 2.3. How deep into the glass wafer the depletion layer extends
will depend on the magnitude of the applied bias and how long it is applied. Initially, during
the bonding, only a few small areas of the wafers will be in contact with each other, but as
the bonding progresses. The depletion zone will create an electric field pulling the wafers
together, and the heating means the surfaces will be able to conform to each other and
the area where the wafers touch will grow over time to encompass the entire surface, thus
creating a permanent bond between the wafers. One of the major defects that can occur
during the bonding process is the formation of voids. These typically appear due to either
large particles on the surface or residual gasses stuck between the wafers. This problem
is shared for all bonding methods. For anodic bonding, these can be easily spotted by
looking through the glass wafer, as Newtonian rings will appear around the voids. [26]
[27]
14
Design, Fabrication and Characterization of a CMUT for a Linear Array Probe
==PAGE:23==
CHAPTER 2. THEORY
Figure 2.3: Overview of the anodic bonding process and the electrical connections to the
glass and silicon wafers. Figure from Comprehensive Microsystems by K. Najafi et al.
[26].
Fusion Bonding
A different bonding method is fusion bonding, where two silicon wafers are bonded to-
gether. The name fusion bonding comes from the fact that the two wafers are fused
together into one, thus forming the bond. [26] However, the requirements for obtaining
fusion bonding are higher than those for anodic bonding. To obtain a fusion bond the two
wafers must first and foremost be polished with a low surface roughness. Any polymer
residues from resist must also have been removed first, along with any other chemical
contaminants on the surface. Hence, the surface of both wafers is chemically active and
can form covalent bonds with each other. To bond the wafers, they must be heated to
higher temperatures at around 900 °C, significantly higher than those in anodic bonding.
[26] This also means the process is incompatible with metals. The strength of the bond in
the fusion bonding process depends heavily on the cleanliness and low roughness of the
surface. These requirements also mean fusion bonding is more susceptible to surface
defects or particles than anodic bonding, and voids can more easily form. [26]
2.5.2
Ion Beam Etching
Normally, dry etching uses plasma and reactive ions to etch away the material. IBE,
or ion beam milling as it is also called, is not reliant on a chemical reaction for etching
to etch away material. Instead, it relies on the bombardment of an inert noble gas ion to
knock off the material to be etched. The process is best compared to sandblasting, where
the bombardment of small particles will remove material. IBE has a high directionality
as it works by ionising noble gas atoms and accelerating them towards the sample with
an electric field, and when the process chamber is kept under a high vacuum to limit
collisions between the accelerated atoms and any residual gasses left in the chamber.
The etching from the ion bombardment means that an IBE system can anisotropically etch
any material, and the etch rate only differs by up to a factor of 3 between materials. [28]
This is a major benefit as it makes it possible to etch even the most exotic materials. Still,
it is also the biggest downside as it makes it difficult to etch patterns as the photoresist or
other layers used to make an etch mask. They are etched at similar rates as the material
to be etched. One way to overcome this is to use thicker layers of etch masks. The
acceleration voltages used to accelerate the ions are typically 500-1000 V as the desired
effect to knock off material and not implant the ions into the sample. [28]
Another downside is that the etched material and the masking layer can be redeposited
elsewhere on the sample instead of being removed. Another problem is when the side-
Design, Fabrication and Characterization of a CMUT for a Linear Array Probe
15
==PAGE:24==
CHAPTER 2. THEORY
walls before the IBE process are tapered. Then, the features can be widened. Trenching
can also occur at the edges of the features where an increased etch rate will give deeper
etches. However, most IBE systems allow for tilting and rotating the wafer to minimise
these effects. [28] For some materials, a reactive gas can be introduced into the chamber
to increase the selectivity of the etching, but it is heavily dependent on the materials to be
etched and can not be used every time. [28]
2.5.3
Low Pressure Chemical Vapour Deposition of Silicon Nitride
For depositing thin films and especially silicon nitride Low Pressure Chemical Vapour
Deposition (LPCVD) is often used as it allows for depositing high quality and uniform
films while also being able to deposit the film on high aspect ratio structures. [29] Before
deposition, the process tube is pumped down and purged with inert nitrogen to remove any
last traces of atmospheric air. The vacuum ensures that the films have less contamination
and that the vacuum also reduces the amount of particles formed. [29] The chemical
reaction for depositing the stoichiometric silicon nitride involves two gasses. dichlorsilane
(DCS) and ammonia. Which at a temperature of around 750 - 800 °C, will react to form
stoichiometric silicon nitride and, as a byproduct, hydrogen and hydrochloric acid. [30]
3 SiH2Cl2 + 4NH3 →Si3 + 6 HCl + 6 H2
(2.27)
This chemical reaction is typically kinetically controlled. [29] This means that the gas flow,
temperature or pressure limits the reaction. For systems with a heated process tube, so-
called hot wall systems, the temperature typically limits the reaction rate of the system;
as a consequence, the temperature must be well controlled as small deviations in the
temperature will give different reaction rates across the boat with wafers in the furnace.
One major downside of a hot wall furnace is that the film will also deposit on the inside of
the chamber, meaning a furnace can only be used for that specific process. The deposition
on the inside of the chamber walls also leads to film flakes falling off with time, which can
cause particle issues in the deposited film. [29] This requires regular cleaning of the
furnace tubes to prevent particle formation in the deposited films. Specifically for nitride,
particle formation can be problematic due to the high stress of the film as silicon nitride
has a tensile stress of ≈1300 MPa. [31] This high stress also means that films thicker
than 200 nm tend to crack, putting an upper limit on the nitride thickness, which can be
deposited. [30] Film flakes can be problematic as they can give pinholes, different etch
rates, lower electrical breakdown, and larger surface roughness. A horizontal furnace
system can be used instead to avoid particles, but they come with a significantly higher
cost. [29]
2.5.4
Gettering
Within commercial MEMS devices where a high level of vacuum is required, certain ma-
terials can trap residual gasses which build up inside hermetically sealed cavities. These
materials are known as getters and are typically included to maintain a controlled ambient
over the lifetime of a device. [32] Depending on the substrate used, this can be even
more crucial, especially since glass substrates can leak gas into hermetically sealed cav-
ities and will require getters to maintain the desired pressure. [32, 33] Getters work by
first adsorping the gasses and diffusion of the adsorbed gas to trap it in the bulk of the
getter. Getters are divided into two categories: evaporable and non-evaporable getters.
Non-evaporable getters are alloys of zirconium, vanadium, iron, aluminium, and various
other metals. [32] Evaporable getters are metal films of calcium, strontium or titanium.
[34] The exact getter used will depend on the gas to be trapped. For anodic bonding, the
glass substrate will outgas oxygen, and Ti is often used to getter the oxygen gas. [34,
16
Design, Fabrication and Characterization of a CMUT for a Linear Array Probe
==PAGE:25==
CHAPTER 2. THEORY
33, 32] Ti also has the benefit of being readily available within cleanrooms. [35] It has
also been proven to give up to a tenfold reduction of the pressure inside an anodically
bonded cavity of glass-silicon when a layer of 300 Å of gold was deposited on top. [35]
This experiment was carried out with a 100 µm deep etched cavity in silicon and with a 1
µm thick layer of titanium, meaning the ratio of volume to titanium was much larger than
what is typically observed in CMUT arrays. However, the amount of atoms a getter can
adsorb and bind in the bulk of it can vary greatly depending on how it is deposited and
treated before bonding and the gas to getter. [36] Thus, a direct parallel to CMUT devices
can not be drawn. However, a rule of thumb for Ti is that one atom of Ti can getter one
gas atom. [36] Meaning the effects of the gettering may be more pronounced for CMUTs
than what was reported for a silicon cavity. Another possible getter proposed is tantalum,
which has been proven to adsorb nitrogen, oxygen and carbon monoxide. [37] However,
it needs to be activated at temperatures over 600 °C before it acts as a getter. [37] In
comparison, titanium will be activated at temperatures starting at 300 °C. [35] Thus, plac-
ing the temperature for activation closer to that which is used during bonding processes,
meaning the bonding and getter activation can be combined. [27, 26]
2.6
Chapter Summary
In this chapter, the important parameters of CMUTs were presented along with the deriva-
tion of the capacitance, pull-in voltage and resonance frequency. Some useful character-
istics of electrical components to help verify the behaviour of fabricated CMUTs. A short
description of the methodology of FEM simulation was also presented, and lastly, selected
topics of the fabrication process were also presented.

Electrical Measurement setups
During the thesis, several types of electrical characterisations were performed using dif-
ferent pieces of equipment to characterise the fabricated wafers electrically. This chapter
will present the pieces of equipment, their purpose, and how they were used for electri-
cal characterisation, along with a short description of the measurement types performed
during this thesis.
3.1
Probe Station

The
MEMS applied sensors group owns a Cas-
cade Summit 12K probe station and probe
needles for making contact with the elements.
This probe station is a semi-automatic probe
station. It can be set up to move the wafer
chuck in a predefined pattern, automatically
addressing entire arrays and wafers based
on predefined measurement locations. The
probe needles only have to be manually set in
the correct location to address the elements
of the arrays, making it possible to perform
long measurements while leaving it unat-
tended. The probe station also has a built-
in microscope to assist in accurately placing
the probe needles and inspecting arrays for
damages. When used, the microscope will
close the built-in EMI shield. This EMI shield
is crucial as the magnitude of the measured
currents, and capacitances are in the pA and
pF range, making the measurements sensi-
tive to outside interference and noise.
It should be noted the EMI shield is only
closed when the microscope is moved down in the position where it is used to inspect the
wafers. The entire probe station is shown in fig. 3.1 where the microscope is in the down
position, and the operator station for the probe station can be seen to the right, where
the chuck can be moved from while using the microscope. To the left in the photo is the
B1500A parameter analyser used for full array characterisation described in section 3.2.
To the right below the operator station is the Agilent Keysight E4990A impedance analyser
used for high-frequency impedance measurements as to be described in section 3.3.
3.2
Parameter Analyser
A B1500 Semiconductor analyser from Agilent performs the wafer-wide characterisation
of whole arrays. It can perform automatic Capacitance-Voltage (CV), Current-Voltage (IV)
and Impedance-frequency (Zf) measurements by being connected to the probe station
and sending instructions for the probe station to move from one element to the next after
performing the measurements. It can even move from array to array automatically. This
18
Design, Fabrication and Characterization of a CMUT for a Linear Array Probe
==PAGE:27==
CHAPTER 3. ELECTRICAL MEASUREMENT SETUPS
automatic behaviour speeds up the time it takes to perform wafer-wide characterisations
and increases reproducibility. The only downside is that the probe station’s movement
needs to be set up carefully since all movements are relative to the starting position. If
set up incorrectly, it will put the probe needles inside the element, potentially destroying it.
However, the layout of the arrays and elements is well-defined from the lithography masks.
So, care must just be taken to set up the movements of the probe station correctly with
the right pitchs.
For each measurement type on the parameter analyser, there is the option of the inte-
gration time. That is the time it will average out the measured values over. It has three
different values: short, medium, and long. For all the measurements in this thesis, the
medium integration time was used, as previous experiences in the MEMS applied sensors
group have shown it to give accurate results but also perform faster measurements than
when the long integration time is used. Prior experience has also shown it essential to
perform a phase, open and short calibration before starting a measurement. Therefore,
these were also performed before any measurement was started. The phase and open
calibrations are done with the probe needles suspended in the air and not having contact
with anything. The short calibration was done by placing the probe needles next to each
other on a large strip of metal next to each other. Thus, a short between the two probes
is made.
Due to the layout of the elements, an array has to be measured twice. First, the elec-
trodes accessible from the bottom of the array were measured, and then the electrodes
accessible from above the array were measured; this design is further described in chap-
ter 5 and chapter 6. This layout meant that the probe used for making contact with the
bottom and top electrodes was changed between the two measurements. In practice,
this was done by switching the cables, connecting the probes to the parameter analyser
and performing a new calibration. The probe needles should be completely identical, but
the calibration was nevertheless performed to rule out the change of probes leading to a
systematic measurement error.
(a) Circuit diagram for the Zf
measurements.
(b) Circuit diagram for the IV
measurements.
(c) Circuit diagram for the CV
measurements,
Figure 3.2: Circuit diagrams for the Zf, IV and CV measurements performed on the param-
eter analyser. The signal probe was placed on the bottom electrode for all measurements
to prevent stray electric fields from influencing the measurements.
3.2.1
Zf Measurement
The purpose of the Zf measurements is to determine if the elements act as capacitors by
sweeping the frequency from 1kHz to 1 MHz in 76 steps. For each frequency step, the
impedance of the element is measured. An Oscillation Voltage (OSC) of 50 mV was used
for the AC bias. The impedance is then plotted in a double logarithmic plot, and according
Design, Fabrication and Characterization of a CMUT for a Linear Array Probe
19
==PAGE:28==
CHAPTER 3. ELECTRICAL MEASUREMENT SETUPS
to the theory described in section 2.3.1, the magnitude of the impedance should decrease
linearly with the frequency, enabling the measurement to quickly screen for good and bad
elements while also finding the capacitance of the element. The circuit diagram of the
measurement setup is shown in fig. 3.2a.
3.2.2
IV Measurement
The purpose of the IV measurements is to screen for short-circuited elements. The mea-
surement is done by applying a DC bias across the element and measuring the current
running through the element. Ideally, the current should be small, and if a large current is
measured, it would indicate a short circuit along the element, which can then be located
along the element using the built-in microscope of the probe station. During this thesis,
the applied bias was increased from 0 to 5 V in steps of 0.5 V and from 0 V to -5 V, also
in steps of 0.5 V. This reverses the field, and then the current running through the ele-
ment can be measured. However, the signal probe is not moved and is always placed on
the bottom electrode. These short circuits have previously been seen to occur during the
anodic bonding process. [17, 9] The circuit diagram for the IV measurements from the
B1500 parameter analyser can be seen in fig. 3.2b.
3.2.3
CV Measurement
The purpose of the CV measurements is to determine how the capacitance changes as
the applied DC bias is applied. The CV curve should be parabolic up to 40% of the pull-
in voltage of the CMUT element. [6] For performing the measurements, the DC bias is
increased to a desired value and then decreased to 0 V. Followed by reducing it to a given
negative bias and then increasing the bias to 0 V again. The sweep is done with a chosen
step size. The size of the step will be a trade-off between the resolution of the final CV
curve and the time it takes to measure, as each step adds two extra measurements to be
performed. During this thesis, a step size of 0.5 V was used to get a good resolution of
the CV curve. For the measurements, a small OSC of 50 mV at a frequency of 50 kHz is
applied on top of the DC bias. Thus, the frequency is far from the MHz range’s resonance
frequency of the elements. The circuit diagram for the CV measurement can be seen in
fig. 3.2c.
3.3
Impedance Measurements
All the impedance measurements done during this thesis were performed using an Agi-
lent Keysight E4990A impedance analyser with a fixed 250 µm pitch ACP40-A-GSG-250
probe. The probe is a ground-signal-ground probe. This configuration means it has two
ground probe needles and a signal probe needle in between them. It has three probe nee-
dles with a fixed pitch of 250 µm. One of the ground needles had previously been bent up
on purpose and disabled, so only one of the ground needles was used during characteri-
sation. By bending it up, it can not go down and touch the element to be measured and,
potentially, destroy it.
The reason for also using a dedicated impedance analyser is its capability to go to even
higher frequencies and use significantly more measurement points. It has a frequency
range from 20 Hz to 120 MHz. It can, for any given frequency sweep, use 1601 mea-
surement points, so for an extended frequency sweep, the points will be spread out with
a large spacing, but by limiting the frequency spectrum, the measurement points will be
spaced more closely together. It can provide an electrical AC bias with a variable OSC for
measurements. During this thesis, an OSC of 200 mV was primarily used. In chapter 4,
an OSC of 50 mV was used for some measurements, and to avoid confusion about which
OSC was used, it will be explicitly stated which OSC was used. The impedance analyser
also can apply a DC bias of up to 42 V. However, this bias is far from high enough, as the
20
Design, Fabrication and Characterization of a CMUT for a Linear Array Probe
==PAGE:29==
CHAPTER 3. ELECTRICAL MEASUREMENT SETUPS
DC bias must reach hundreds of volts to reach the designed pull-in voltages and go be-
yond them. Therefore, the impedance analyser is combined with a Keithley 2410 source
meter, and they are connected to a bias-T made in the MEMS applied sensors group,
allowing for a higher DC bias during the measurements than the impedance analyser can
deliver.
3.3.1
Resonance Determination
The phase angle and the magnitude of the impedance will have a peak at the resonance
frequency when determining the unbiased resonance frequency. Otherwise, the phase
angle should be -90 °. Therefore, the resonance frequency can be found when measur-
ing the impedance and plotting the phase angle and the magnitude of the impedance in
the same plot. However, when determining the resonance frequency, it is essential to
remember that any applied bias will induce the spring-softening effect as mentioned in
chapter 2 and decrease the resonance frequency. Therefore, to measure the unbiased
resonance frequency of the DUT. The applied DC bias would preferably be 0. However,
the resonance peak might not be visible due to the absence of the spring-softening effect.
Applying a small DC bias gives the spring-softening effect, and a resonance peak will
appear. Therefore, to determine the unbiased resonance frequency, a small bias must
be applied; it must be kept as low as possible to limit the spring softening effect from af-
fecting the measurement of the unbiased resonance frequency too much. The same care
is unnecessary for resonance frequencies at the operation bias. Instead, the desired DC
bias can be applied, and the phase angle peak can be determined directly.
3.3.2
Pull-In Determination
To determine the pull-in voltage of the DUT. The frequency is swept as when determin-
ing the resonance frequency. However, the DC bias is increased from measurement to
measurement. When the DC bias is increased, the resonance frequency will decrease,
and the peak phase angle value should increase, as well as result from the spring soft-
ening effect. Pull-in is characterised by a large downshift in the resonance frequency of
the DUT. The phase angle peak should also widen drastically from a single well-defined
peak to a broad peak in the frequency domain. This behaviour will be apparent when
comparing multiple values of the DC bias next to each other, as it can be challenging to
determine from a single measurement if the peak is widened or not. An alternative way
to determine pull-in is from the current running through the DUT at pull-in. The sudden
decrease in the distance between the two electrodes will increase the capacitance, and a
small current will run into the DUT to store the additional charges. The capacitance can
also be measured to increase at the same time.
3.3.3
Stability Measurement
The stability measurements test how stable the DUT is over a prolonged period when
a DC bias and AC bias are applied, meaning if the DUT is stable enough for use in a
final linear array probe. The stability measurement procedure is to increase the DC bias
from 0 to a desired value and then decrease it to 0 V with a fixed voltage step size.
Each value of the DC bias is then held for 10 minutes, where continuous measurements
are performed for the entire time. A prolonged measurement will show the capacitance,
peak phase angle, resonance frequency, and magnitude of the impedance drift over 10
minutes due to charging effects. Thus showing if the DUT could be used for a prolonged
period as would be required in a final probe used in a clinical setting. Currently, there is
no fixed requirement for how much the capacitance can drop over time. The important
metric is that the capacitance and phase angle will drop to a stable level and not decrease
further. For the stability measurements, the applied DC bias should never be higher than
the intended operation bias of the array. The measurements are not designed to find the
Design, Fabrication and Characterization of a CMUT for a Linear Array Probe
21
==PAGE:30==
CHAPTER 3. ELECTRICAL MEASUREMENT SETUPS
breaking point of the DUT. Instead, it is to show the array can be operated for prolonged
periods in a scanner and probe.

along with the different electrical measurement types used and how they were performed.

This chapter will provide an overview of the electrical characterisation performed on al-
ready fabricated wafers and the experiences learned from the previously fabricated an-
odically bonded CMUT wafers where two different ways of defining the top electrodes
were used before the new design was made. The differences between the previously
processed wafers and the implications for the new design will be presented.
4.1
Necessity of Initial Characterisation

03 or the PES etched wafer, and the wafer

performance between the wafers will be essential to determine which of the two etching
methods should be preferred during this thesis, as any damage to the electrodes at the
end of the processing will be detrimental to the performance of the fabricated arrays. The
use of PES to etch the top electrodes had not been attempted before in the MEMS-applied
sensors group for fabricating anodically bonded CMUTs. Thus, the effect on the electrical
performance was not known beforehand. The PES Aluminium (Al) etchant is reported
to etch silicon nitride. However, the exact etch rate is not known.[38] The operation of
the CMUT arrays depends on the nitride bridge to create an insulating layer between the
top and bottom electrodes. Any defects in this layer can lead to electrical arching and

the burning of the arrays during electrical characterisation as upwards of 200 volts are

4.2
Characterisation of PES Etched Wafer

The first wafer to be characterised was
wafer 03, which had the top electrode
etched using PES by submerging it into
PES for 30 minutes. The processing was
done by Connor P. P. Gore and Moiz A.
Talib, and it is covered in their bachelor

[18] After the etching, they no-
ticed many CMUT elements had lost vac-
uum due to over-etching.[18] However, the
wafer had shown good electrical perfor-
mance, but they reported it suffered from
charging. [18] As there are tens of arrays
and several thousand elements on a sin-
gle wafer. It has been estimated by PhD
Stine L. G. Pedersen, who has fabricated
a similar design, that a complete wafer-
level characterisation will take 31 consec-
utive days of characterisation. [14] There-
fore, only a select few elements were char-
acterised in the previous work and during
this thesis.
For all the characterisations
described in this section, the naming of the arrays follows the convention shown in fig. 4.1
where both an overview of the design layout and the numbering for each array is given.
For wafer 03, it was decided to focus on the test arrays near the 11-1 384-element lin-
ear array since it was reported to have the highest number of working elements on the
wafer by C. Gore and M. Talib [18]. The measurements were performed by characteris-
ing elements in the adjacent 16 element 340 µm pitch test array and 65 cell test element
and performing impedance measurements to determine the pull-in and stability of the de-
sign. However, no capacitance was measured for most characterised elements, and a
high phase angle was measured. The current also seemed to flow freely through the el-
ements. This behaviour indicated that the elements were not operating as capacitors but
closer to a resistor. An example of an impedance measurement from an element where
no capacitance was measured and the current seemed to flow freely through it is shown in
fig. 4.2. One key thing to notice from this plot is the impedance magnitude, the left y-axis,
and the phase angle, the right y-axis. As described in section 2.3, a capacitor should
have a phase angle of -90◦with the only exception being at the resonance of the CMUT,
and the magnitude of the impedance should not increase with the frequency. The phase
angle behaviour is thus far from what would be expected from a capacitor and a CMUT.
Instead, it seems to have a behaviour closer to an inductor.
Normal working elements were also found on the PES etched wafer, where the electrical
behaviour was as expected from a capacitor and CMUT array. Such an example can be
seen in fig. 4.3 where when a DC bias of 0 volts is applied, there are no resonance peaks,
but the phase angle is still at nearly 90◦. A resonance peak for the phase angle occurred
when a DC bias was applied to give the spring softening effect. Ideally, there should
only be one peak, as all the CMUT cells should have the same resonance frequency.
24
Design, Fabrication and Characterization of a CMUT for a Linear Array Probe
==PAGE:33==
CHAPTER 4. INITIAL CHARACTERISATION
However, that is the ideal scenario, which is not always accurate due to non-uniformities
from processing leading to a difference in plate thickness across an array, electrode size
and gap height. These minor differences can lead to them having different resonance
frequencies. Which either broadens the peak or creates smaller secondary peaks. How-
ever, when increasing the applied DC bias, they should be more closely grouped around
the resonance peak and go to a lower frequency. In fig. 4.3, the two extra peaks are at
24 and 28 MHz, at a relatively higher frequency than the central peak at around 18 MHz.
This significant difference will unlikely have been caused by process variations during the
fabrication, but it can not be ruled out. This behaviour has also been noticed in previ-
ous work in the MEMS-applied sensors group where a LOCOS based fabrication process
was used to fabricate the CMUT arrays. [15] Attention should be paid to whether this
resonance peak also appears for the wafers fabricated during this thesis.

Figure 4.3: The phase angle and total impedance plotted as a function of the frequency
