around 18 MHz. In the two plots, the scale of the phase angle is different.
Many other elements characterised on the PES etched wafers also exploded during char-
acterisations, even at low applied biases. Therefore, the quality of the nitride film can be
questioned and could likely have been etched by the PES solution when the top electrode
was etched. This over-etching of the nitride would lead to worse insulation between the
two electrodes and result in elements exploding during characterisations. Some function-
ing elements were found. However, increasing the applied DC bias to more than 60 V
resulted in the elements exploding. After noticing this behaviour, it was decided to move
on to the next wafer, where the top electrode was etched using PES to see the difference
in performance and see if it was a general issue with the nitride or if it was the PES which
had attacked the nitride.
4.3
Characterisation of Ion Beam Etched Wafer
For the IBE etched wafer, wafer 07. C. Gore and M. Talib found that the array with the
highest amount of working elements was array 4-2.[18] Like with the PES etched wafer
the nearby 16 element 340 µm pitch test array was characterised first to avoid damaging
the 384-element linear array 4-2. For this test array, all 16 elements were characterised.
Design, Fabrication and Characterization of a CMUT for a Linear Array Probe
25
==PAGE:34==
CHAPTER 4. INITIAL CHARACTERISATION
The measurements were performed using an OSC of 50 mV and a DC bias of 0 to 60 V
in steps of 10V over the frequency range from 1 MHz to 30 MHz. Out of all 16 elements,
only three were found to behave as capacitors. The remaining 13 elements had a peak
phase angle from -12◦to -35◦. With the majority being between -12◦and -25◦. Far from
the -90◦of a capacitor. The three working elements were then measured again by in-
creasing the applied DC bias, measuring with a bias of 0 V and then from 60 V to 220 V
in 10 V increments. One of the three elements exploded at 60 V, indicating that it barely
understood the initial measurement. The remaining two elements withstood the full 220
V. This meant that only 1/8 of the elements worked and could go to the designed pull-in
voltage. This ratio of working elements is much lower than reported by C. Gore and M.
Talib, in their BSc thesis, where it was stated that there was a rate of working elements
of around 15/16 for the neighbouring 384-element linear array. [18] While also being far
from the wafer-wide amount of working element ratio of a little over 1/2, which they found.
[18]
It was decided to investigate if this was a case of having chosen a bad test array by
characterising four elements from the nearby 384-element linear array 4-2 where they had
found a high ratio of working elements. The four elements measured were elements 1,
25, 41 and 69. All four elements were measured using an OSC of 50 mV in the frequency
range of 1 MHz to 30 MHz and with a DC bias from 0 to 60 V in 10 V increments. For all
elements, except for element 41, the phase angle was far from the expected -90◦, and
they had a current running freely through them, indicating that they weren’t capacitors. A
comparison of a bad element, element 25, and a working element, element 41, is shown
in fig. 4.4.
As element 41 was found to be functioning as expected from a CMUT element, it was
measured again with an increased DC bias, starting with 0 V and then from 60 V to 220
V in steps of 10 V. As element 41 continued to perform as expected it was decided to do
one last measurement at 0 V and then at a DC bias of 220 V, 230 V and then at 240 V.
It survived all these biases. However, it was not observed that it would go into pull-in.
At such high biasses, pull-in was expected as the designed pull-in bias for the elements
was 200 V, and at 240 V, it operated much above the pull-in voltage. This behaviour
would indicate the pull-in bias is much higher than the designed pull-in of 200 V. As 240
V already is a high bias to apply over such a short distance, it was decided not to try and
increase the bias further as this would likely mean the element would explode. Instead,
a stability measurement of the element was performed to see if the capacitance would
change over time during repeated measurements. This stability measurement will be
described in section 4.3.1. It was also decided to characterise 16 element 340 µm pitch
test array 11. As can be seen from fig. 4.1, the 384-element linear array 11 is far away
from array 4, and it should indicate if the behaviour is the same across the entire wafer or
if it is limited to a small part of the wafer that the elements are not behaving as intended.
4.3.1
Stability of Array 4 Element 41
For element 41 in 384-element linear array 4-2, the stability measurement was done at
voltages of 0 and then from 100 to 220 V in 10 V increments. It was found that the element
was stable up to 200 V, where a slight drop in the capacitance was noticed over the 10
minutes. Starting at 6.31 pF and decreasing to 6.29 pF, a change of -0.32 % over the
entire 10 minutes was seen and can be seen in fig. 4.5a. When the bias was increased
to 220 V, the elements survived the first nine measurements, and then, during the 10th
measurement, it exploded. The exact moment for the explosion can even be seen in the
frequency sweep as the phase angle suddenly increased to be around 0◦. All subsequent
frequency sweeps for 220 V then showed a near-zero phase angle. From the frequency
26

Despite the low number of working elements, this low relative change is a good result,
indicating when the elements did work. They were stable, even at the designed pull-in
voltage of 200 V.
4.3.2
Characterisation of Array 11
The procedure for characterising 16 element 340 µm pitch test array 11 was the same
as for 16 element 340 µm pitch test array 4. The frequency range was from 1MHz to 30
MHz, and for the initial characterisations, a bias from 0 V to 60 V in 10 V increments was
used, and a total of five working elements were found. Namely elements 9, 13, 2, 6 and 8.
However, during the 10 V DC bias measurement for element 8, a slight poof was heard,
and the capacitance and phase angle dropped to zero. It went from operating as a ca-
pacitor to operating as a resistor. Thus, only four elements kept working up to 60 V. One
interesting peculiarity noted for the working elements was that elements 9 and 13 had a
capacitance of approximately 6.3 pF and elements 2 and 6 both had a capacitance of 7.9
pF. This difference is not a large absolute difference but a significant relative difference.
This difference is interesting as the arrays should be designed identically and thus should
have the same capacitance. The only difference between the two measurements is from
the probing. The signal probe was placed on the bottom electrode for the odd-numbered
elements, and for the even-numbered elements, the signal probe was placed on the top
electrode. The different placements of the signal probe could affect the measured capac-
itance, as having the signal on the top electrode can make the measurement more sus-
ceptible to stray electric fields. These differences in capacitances mean the placement
of the signal pin must be considered in future characterisations during the characterisa-
tion of array 4. None of the working elements was even numbered, and the suspicion of
the effect of the signal probe placement could not be verified on that array. Plots of the
impedance magnitude and phase angle can be seen in fig. 4.9 for elements 6, 10 and
13. The similarities between the two functioning elements, 6 and 13, can be seen despite

carried out and is described in section 4.4.
4.4
Effect of OSC voltage on Noise
As mentioned in section 4.3.2 at lower frequencies, a significant noise level was observed
for the impedance measurements. Thus, a smaller study was conducted where the OSC
was increased to examine if the Signal-to-Noise Ratio (SNR) could be increased and
obtain less noisy measurements. The increase of the OSC leading to lower noise had
initially been seen to lower the amount of noise in the measurements by PhD Stine L. G.
Pedersen. [14] Increasing it will increase the oscillation of the top plate and should give a
28
Design, Fabrication and Characterization of a CMUT for a Linear Array Probe
==PAGE:37==

Figure 4.9: Impedance measurement for array 11 340 µm for elements 6, 10 and 11 from

higher impedance signal and as a result, increasing the SNR. The same DC biasses were
used to keep the measurements as comparable as possible. For studying the effect of
OSC on the noise, a stability measurement was performed on element 6 from 16 element
340 µm pitch test array with a DC bias going to +60, then -60 V in 5 V increments. It was
intended to go from -60 V to 0 V, but the voltage sweep had been set up incorrectly, and
the voltage was not increased from -60 V to 0 V. The small step size will give a higher
resolution, which will be needed to determine if increasing the OSC increases the SNR.
However, the instrument did not go to 0 V after measuring -60 V, and attention should paid
to it for future measurements. First, element 6 was measured using an OSC of 50 mV.
Then, the measurement was repeated 24 hours later with an OSC of 200 mV to allow any
charging effects to decrease and keep the measurements as identical as possible. The
voltage sweep error of not going from 60 V to 0 V was intentionally carried over to keep the
measurements as identical as possible. The two CV curves can be seen in fig. 4.10 where
the axes are scaled to be identical. The key things to pay attention to are the standard
deviations and the magnitude of the error bars for the 50 mV OSC measurement. The
error bars are much larger, and the phase angle measurements especially have error
bars so large that a -60 V, 60 V or 0 V measurement can not be statistically discerned
from each other. These large standard deviations are not the case for the 200 mV OSC
measurements, where the error bars are so small they can barely be seen as more than
points. This reduction in standard deviation indicates that an OSC of 200 mV is better as
it lowers the noise in the signal when performing impedance measurements. For future
reference, a short measurement documentation was also written for the MEMS-applied
sensors groups’ internal wiki. It can be seen in appendix B.
4.5
High Voltage Stability of Element
After determining that using a 200 mV OSC, a stability measurement going to a higher
DC bias was performed on wafer 7, on element 6 in the 16 element 340 µm pitch test
array 11, element 6 to see how the stability of an element was when the SNR had been
increased. The measurement was done by starting with a DC bias of 0 V, then increasing
it to 200 V, decreasing it to -200 V and then increasing it to 0 V in 20 V increments.
Unfortunately, the element exploded when -100 V was applied the first time, so only the

29
==PAGE:38==

(a) Stability measurement when using 50 mV

Figure 4.10: Comparison of using an OSC of 50 mV and 200 mV in stability measure-
ments. The DC voltage was increased from 0 V to 60 V, then decreased to - 60 V in
steps of 5 volts at a time, and each step held for 10 minutes. The first measurement was
performed with an OSC of 50 mV and then with an OSC of 200 mV 24 hours later.
positive voltages will be discussed in the following discussion. The behaviour following the
explosion was still consistent with a capacitor. However, the capacitance was low, in the 1-
2 pF range, with no resonance peaks. The capacitor behaviour was likely from the severed
connection after the explosion, which created a gap in the bottom electrode, which will be
a capacitor in series with the element and decrease the overall capacitance. The plot of
the capacitance and the peak phase angle can be seen plotted as a function of the applied
DC bias in fig. 4.11a. Here, it can be seen the capacitance follows the same trend as in
fig. 4.10, and the error bars are still small at the low voltages. They increase when going to
the larger values of the applied DC bias. Still, this behaviour would also be expected as a
higher DC bias, which means the spring softening effect will be more considerable. Which
in turn could lead to a larger standard deviation. Charging could also affect the stability
over time as it will decrease the capacitance for prolonged measurements. Significant
charging of CMUTs can also be observed in fig. 4.11a where there is a capacitance and
peak phase difference between the first and second measurements at the same DC bias.
The standard deviations for the two curves are also different.
When examining the capacitance and peak phase angle plotted as a function of time in
fig. 4.11b, the charging effects are apparent and will explain the large standard deviations.
Thus, considerations for limiting the charging should be taken in the new design of a
CMUT array. Reducing the pull-in voltage should be considered so the operation voltage
will be at lower voltages where charging is not pronounced could be a possible solution.
It should also be noted that the 200V DC is the designed pull-in voltage of the CMUTs.
Thus, they were never intended to be operated at such high voltages in normal operation.
However, from fig. 4.11b, charging is seen already at 120 V, below the designed 160
V operation voltage. As a consequence, the operation voltage should be reduced to be
below 120 V. With this last high voltage stability measurement, the initial characterisations
performed were concluded as the majority of what could be learned from the previous
batch of wafers had been learned while also keeping enough time to design, fabricate
and characterise a new batch of wafers.

4.6
Chapter Summary
In this chapter, the initial characterisations performed on two differently processed wafers
were described, and what could be learned from them before starting a new design of
anodic bonded wafers. It was determined that the best way to separate the top electrodes
is by IBE, as the wet etchant seemed to have damaged the insulating oxy-nitride layer
between the electrodes. It would also be best to have an operation voltage lower than
120 V to limit the charging of the CMUTs. The effect of the OSC was also examined, and
it was found that increasing the OSC from 50 mV to 200 mV significantly increased the
SNR.
Design, Fabrication and Characterization of a CMUT for a Linear Array Probe
31
==PAGE:40==
5
Design
This chapter aims to describe specifications for final CMUT arrays produced during this
thesis and the design process. The chapter will include justifications for the design spec-
ifications, how the CMUT dimensions were chosen to meet the outlined specifications
and finally, how these design parameters were implemented in lithography masks and a
discussion of the simulation models used.
5.1
Design Specification
For this master thesis, the aim was to, first and foremost, design a CMUT for a linear
array probe. To design such a probe, several considerations need to be made. These
range from and are not limited to the final function, the available tools and materials,
how to perform electrical characterisation, and how it can be packaged into a final probe.
As such, one has to start somewhere and decide on at least one of the final CMUTs
parameters. In this thesis, the parameter that was decided to be fixed from the start was
the resonance frequency. From there, the remaining parameters and dimensions were
determined in the following order:
1. Frequency of the emitted ultrasound wave
2. The pitch of the linear elements. Must satisfy λ/2 pitch from the centre frequency
3. Number of CMUT elements and amount of CMUT cells in one element
4. Selecting the pull-in voltage, Vpi, which must be compatible with the scanner and
dielectric layers
5. Radius of cells so they fit in the elements, determination of cell radius, a.
6. Determine plate thickness, h, to obtain desired frequency when immersed
7. Determining the vacuum gap, g, that will give the desired pull-in voltage
8. Performing FEM simulations to confirm the found parameters and dimensions meet
the requirements
5.1.1
Frequency
In this thesis, the submersion frequency was chosen to be 15 MHz. A 15 MHz submersion
frequency would allow the designed and fabricated CMUT arrays to be compared to sim-
ilar CMUT linear arrays fabricated using a different LOCOS based process. The LOCOS
based CMUT arrays have been fabricated and characterised in earlier work in the MEMS
applied sensors group by PhD Stine L. G. Pedersen and Msc.Eng. Søren W. Gjaldbæk
and have an immersion frequency of 15 MHz.[14] [15] Meaning that the comparison would
show the differences that come from using a different fabrication method and materials
in the arrays. Thus, the design should be as similar as possible to the LOCOS based
design.
5.1.2
λ/2 Pitch of Arrays
For the second requirement, the λ/2 pitch is a requirement from the collaborating research
section at Center for Fast Ultrasound (CFU) as this pitch will push the directionality of the
grating lobes to -90 °. Thus, they are absent in the emitted ultrasound and do not result
in imaging artefacts. [39] [40] Grating lobes are an undesired effect from the periodicity
32
Design, Fabrication and Characterization of a CMUT for a Linear Array Probe
==PAGE:41==
CHAPTER 5. DESIGN
of the transducer and can lead to off-axis imaging artefacts and ghost echoes in the fi-
nal ultrasound image. [9] For this design, the immersion frequency should be 15 MHz,
as mentioned earlier in section 5.1.1. The pitch is found from the relationship between
the frequency, wavelength, and speed of propagation in a medium. The wavelength in
water can be determined from the speed of propagation and the desired frequency of the
ultrasound wave. For ultrasound waves in water at ambient temperature, the speed of
propagation is 1540 m/s [41]. Thus, the wavelength will be
λ = vwater
fcenter
= 1540 m/s
15 MHz = 51.33µm,
(5.1)
and the pitch of the design must be 51.33 µm. to satisfy the λ/2 criterion. With the pitch
fixed, the remaining parameters can be determined.
5.1.3
Number of CMUT Elements and Cells In One Element
The amount of CMUT elements and the number of cells in one element was for this de-
sign already predefined as it would need to match the pre-existing LOCOS based CMUT
design to keep them as similar as possible allowing for the comparison of the two. There-
fore, for this thesis, the designed CMUT arrays will have 384 linear elements, each with 65
CMUT cells, so the design will fit into already designed PCBs to connect the arrays to the
acoustical measurement setup and the ultrasound scanner at CFU. Normally, the number
of elements will be a trade-off between the pressure (signal) and the bandwidth. Which
one to favour will, therefore, be dependent on the specified application of the transducer
the array is used in. [42][14]
5.1.4
Selection Of Pull-in Voltage
For determining the pull-in voltage of the CMUTs, the maximal value possible was 200V,
as this is the maximal bias the scanner available at CFU can deliver. There is no reason
to create a design where the pull-in voltage is much higher than the maximum applicable
bias from the scanner. However, for this design, the pull-in voltage was decided to be set
even lower at 125 V. This builds upon the knowledge gained from the characterisations of
previously fabricated wafers described in chapter 4 where the insulating layers were not
able to withstand the higher biases at 200 V for prolonged time. Also, in other work in the
MEMS applied sensors group, charging had been observed at DC biases higher than 100
V as described earlier in section 4.5 and in earlier work. [9] Reducing the pull-in voltage
to 125 V means that the operating DC bias at 80% of the pull-in voltage will be 100 V, as
also seen in chapter 4. Thus, the problems of being unable to determine the experimental
pull-in voltage and the effects of charging will hopefully be avoided. The lower pull-in
voltage will mean the gap height must be smaller, as seen from eq. (2.11). However, this
should not be an issue as the remaining dimensions can also be chosen while keeping
the smaller pull-in voltage in mind. For this, FEM simulations were performed to find the
optimal dimensions.
5.2
Simulations To Find The Desired CMUT Dimensions
For finding the desired CMUT cell dimensions, such as the radius, top-plate thickness
and gap height, FEM simulations were performed to find the optimal parameters for a λ/2
pitch design at 15 MHz resonance in PDMS. As described in section 2.2.3, the reason
for performing FEM simulations is that the top plate does not consist of a single layer. In-
stead, it is a combination of four different materials, namely silicon nitride, silicon dioxide,
aluminium, and poly-silicon, and each of these has its inherent stresses, which can be
Design, Fabrication and Characterization of a CMUT for a Linear Array Probe
33
==PAGE:42==
CHAPTER 5. DESIGN
either tensile or compressive and have different orders of magnitude. Therefore, the top-
plate deflection, pull-in voltage and resonance frequency can be challenging to calculate
analytically using the theory described in chapter 2. To find the correct dimensions, FEM
simulations were performed instead of using the analytical model from chapter 2, although
these simulations build upon the theory presented in chapter 2. For doing the FEM simu-
lations, two COMSOL models were used. One was a MATLAB/COMSOL model based on
materials from a LOCOS based fabrication process where a MATLAB script was used to
build the COMSOL models, run them and return the result. From now on, this model will
be called the MATLAB/COMSOL model. The second model was an anodic-based COM-
SOL model where everything was defined through COMSOL. This model will be called
the COMSOL model from now on. The main difference between them is the geometry
and materials used for the substrate. At the beginning of this thesis, it was assumed that
these two models were identical and would yield the same result. Unfortunately, they did
not; the two models gave different results for identical structures and were subsequently
found to be remarkably different. The investigation into the difference between the two
models will be covered in detail in section 5.3 and how their geometry is set up. For find-
ing the desired dimensions, it was decided that the COMSOL model would be the optimal
one, as it matched the CMUTs final structure most accurately. It could also be used to
calculate the frequency of the emitted ultrasound in water with a layer of PDMS on top.
This is something the MATLAB/COMSOL model could not do directly. Instead, it used the
theoretical conversion Lamb model in eq. (2.12) to find the submersion frequency from
the simulated resonance frequency in air.
5.2.1
Simulation: Setup and Results

a top-plate thickness of 4.5 µm. This design has the aspect ratio hLOCOS/aLOCOS = 4.5
µm/22.3 µm≈0.20, and for this design, the Lamb model could accurately predict the res-
onance frequency. [14] So the high aspect ratio does not mean the Lamb model can’t be
trusted for the new design and still may be valid.

5.3
Difference Between Models
As mentioned initially, the two models used in this thesis were assumed to be identical.
However, this was not the case. Initially, for what was thought to be the same geom-
etry, the difference between the MATLAB/COMSOL model and the COMSOL model for
the unbiased resonance frequency was more than 3 MHz. This difference meant it wasn’t
obvious which model to trust, making choosing the design parameters impossible. There-
fore, time was spent examining the models and what made them give different values for
the pull-in voltage and unbiased resonance frequency. To verify which of the two models
would agree with experimentally found values the best. They were compared to the ex-
perimental results of a λ/2 pitch 15 MHz design based on a LOCOS process, which had
been previously designed, fabricated and characterised in the MEMS applied sensors
group. [14] [15]
5.3.1
Comparison Of Models For A LOCOS Design
The LOCOS design used for comparison was as close as possible to the glass-based
design developed in this thesis. The LOCOS based design had a cell radius of 22.3 µm,
a top-plate height of 4.5 µm, a gap-height of 66.0 nm and a clamping distance of 5 µm.
These dimensions were then used to calculate the pull-in voltage, unbiased frequency

the differences are less than 0.5 MHz. Of course, it would ideally be zero, but since the
calculations are based on COMSOL FEM simulations where especially the mesh or how

is insufficient to discard one of the two models. For a CMUT array with the same dimen-
sions, it was found by Stine L. G. Pedersen that the frequency when cast in PDMS and
characterised in water was around 15 MHz at 200-220V DC-bias. [14] This bias is close
to the designed pull-in voltage for the CMUT cell. Still, pull-in was not observed at 220V or
for higher applied biases [14], meaning that the resonance frequency when encapsulated
in PDMS and characterised in water at pull-in could not be experimentally determined.
Still, the resonance frequency at 200-220 V DC-bias can be used to compare with the
FEM models to verify that they can predict the same resonance frequency as the one
measured experimentally. Based on the designed pull-in and operating voltage, the two
COMSOL models came closer, for a given bias, than the MATLAB/COMSOL model. How-
ever, it is impossible to say which of the two models is the best. The two COMSOL models
also predicted a secondary resonance peak at 13.2 MHz. The substrate thickness was
increased to attempt to remove the extra peak, but it still remained. The extra resonance
peak is theorised to be substrate ringing, reflection or a similar artefact from the COMSOL
simulation, which was unfortunately not able to be explained fully from work done during
this thesis and will require further investigation. As no apparent optimal model could be
found using the LOCOS based design, it was decided to investigate further why the two
models gave such different results for the anodic design.
5.3.2
Comparison Of Models For An Anodic-Based Design
It was impossible to determine if the MATLAB/COMSOL or the COMSOL-based model
was the best to predict experimental values for a LOCOS design. It was decided further
to investigate the differences between the two models. As the MATLAB/COMSOL model
generated and saved the underlying COMSOL files it used for its simulations, these were
opened, and the geometry, materials, and the mesh used were compared to the anodic-
based COMSOL model. One of the things the two models shared was the boundary
conditions, which had been set up in the same way. However, numerous differences
were found, ranging from the mesh used in the simulation to the materials and geometry.
The MATLAB/COMSOL model did not use materials for an anodic design. Instead, it used
substrates and materials which resembled a LOCOS based design. This also explains
the similarity of the MATLAB/COMSOL and the LOCOS COMSOL model. A schematic
comparison of the two different geometries used in the anodic COMSOL model and the
MATLAB/COMSOL model can be seen in fig. 5.2. The distances used to clamp the top
plate and the substrate thickness are not shown in fig. 5.2. All individual differences were
slowly changed in the anodic-based COMSOL model to match the geometry in the MAT-
36
Design, Fabrication and Characterization of a CMUT for a Linear Array Probe
==PAGE:45==
CHAPTER 5. DESIGN
LAB/COMSOL model. Simulate the resonance frequency of the CMUT in water and air
after each change to see its effect and how close the two models now were. The list of
all the changes and differences for the unbiased resonance frequency in air was:
• Difference in clamping distance (1.5 MHz)
• Different bottom substrate used (Si for the MATLAB/COMSOL model) (0.7 MHz)
• Bottom electrode was part of the Si substrate (≈0.1 MHz)
• Difference in the placement of SiO2 (0.3 MHz)
• Coarser mesh in the MATLAB/COMSOL model (0.2 MHz)
• Thinner substrate in the MATLAB/COMSOL model (0.3 MHz)

Figure 5.2: Comparison of the geometries in the COMSOL and MATLAB-COMSOL
model. None of the models are drawn to scale. It can be seen that different materials
were used for the two models. When computing the unbiased resonance frequency in
air, the PDMS and water layer are not included.
Here, the most significant contributing factor was how the two models defined the clamp-
ing distance differently. The MATLAB/COMSOL model has two distances. However, only
one distance is used when it generates the geometry for calculating the resonance fre-
quency. These two distances were almost identical for the LOCOS based design; thus,
the difference was not noticed when comparing the two models. The difference in the
clamping distance was accounted for in the comparison for the LOCOS based structure in
section 5.3.1 as this otherwise would have been misleading when comparing the two mod-
els. However, the two distances were not as similar for the anodic-based design, leading
to a difference in the unbiased resonance frequency of 1.5 MHz when the same clamp-
ing distance was used in the COMSOL model as in the MATLAB/COMSOL model. This
large difference highlights the importance of using the correct clamping distance when

Table 5.3: Resonances for the anodic-based design simulated with both models. The
dimensions used was a=22.3 µm, h=4.5 µm, g=66.0 nm.
setting up simulations. Secondly, the substrate change also showed a significant effect
of 0.7 MHz. This pronounced effect was initially not anticipated to have such a significant
effect. Still, when considering the aspect ratios, an almost 5 µm thick top plate is bonded
over a cavity with a gap height of a little under 90 nm. Then, how the stress spreads
into the substrate should affect the resonance of the top plate. How the stress spreads in
the substrate will depend on the substrate materials, as silicon and silica glass will have
different mechanical properties. Likewise, the substrate thickness will also influence this.
If the substrate is too thin, the stress can not spread down into the substrate in the same
way as if a thick substrate was used in the model. The most accurate model would have
the full thickness of the substrate, but at some point, the effect would also be expected to
drop off as the substrate is around 500 µm thick while the cavities are under 1 µm thick.
A substrate thickness of 30 µwas used for all the simulations, which is six times thicker
than the top plate and should allow for adequate spreading of the stress in the model.
A study on the exact thickness needed was not performed due to time constraints, but it
would be beneficial to investigate this in future work. The difference in the placement of
the SiO2 layer and its impact on the resonance frequency is also explained by the stress
propagation in the different materials. One of the last things that was changed was the
mesh size in the COMSOL model, as COMSOL does FEM simulations. The mesh dimen-
sions will be able to impact the results obtained significantly. So, it is unsurprising that
making the mesh coarser would also change the results. Generally, as fine a mesh as
possible is always desired for FEM simulations, but a finer mesh also means longer com-
putational times. A mesh convergence study was not performed as the same mesh used
was shown to give an accurate result for the LOCOS COMSOL model. Still, depending
on the results from the fabrication and characterisations, it would be recommended for
future work. Making it a trade-off between accuracy and how long it takes to get a result.
Considering all of this, the most reliable model for the anodic process was deemed to be
the COMSOL model. This model reflects the final geometry, with the proper materials,
as closely as possible and thus should give more accurate results for the resonance fre-
quency and pull-in voltage. Therefore, it was decided to use the new COMSOL model
to simulate the dimensions needed to obtain the 15 MHz biased resonance frequency in
PDMS.
5.4
Designed Arrays and Lithography Masks
During this thesis, two sets of lithography masks were designed for the anodic bonding
process. The CMUT dimensions for both mask sets were identical and were those de-
scribed earlier and can be found in table 5.1. The key difference was whether the top
or bottom electrodes were common for the entire array. In previous work in the MEMS
applied sensors group, all anodic-based designs have had a common top electrode, and
all LOCOS based designs have had a common bottom electrode. This difference should
not impact the final function of the CMUT arrays. However, to be able to rule this out
when comparing the two fabrication methods completely, it was necessary to have both
a common top electrode and a common bottom electrode CMUT arrays with identical
38
Design, Fabrication and Characterization of a CMUT for a Linear Array Probe
==PAGE:47==
CHAPTER 5. DESIGN
dimensions. Otherwise, any difference could be due to the difference in common elec-
trodes. To design the two sets of lithography masks as similar as possible, the mask set
where the top electrode was common for the entire array was drawn and finalised first,
and then the set was converted to a common bottom electrode. The reason for doing it
in this order was that an initial set of lithography masks with a common top electrode had
been made earlier and used to fabricate the wafers characterised in chapter 4. [16] Thus,
the dimensions could be changed to those found in section 5.2 without having to make all
the lithography masks from new and afterwards, the more extensive changes needed to
make a common bottom electrode design could be implemented.
5.4.1
Changes Made To the Existing Lithography Masks
In the new design, the most notable changes were to implement the dimensions for the
CMUT cells found in section 5.2. These parameters meant that the radius of the cavity
was reduced by 4 µm to be 21 µm, and the radius of the bottom electrode was increased
by 200 nm to be 19 µm. The bottom electrode could be increased by 200 nm as the old
design had a 9 µm between the edge of the cavity and the electrode to give a forgiving
margin of safety when performing the alignment of subsequent lithography steps. The
increase of the electrode radius will lead to an increased capacitance of the CMUT cells
as found in section 2.2.1 in eq. (2.6) as well as a decreased resistance. Thus, the signal
from one element should be larger as well. Increasing the signal-to-noise ratio makes
the CMUT cells perform better. [44] The change in the radii also meant the interconnects
within the elements had to be extended to ensure electrical connection between all the
CMUT cells.
While designing the new lithography masks, it was discovered that the 384-element arrays
in the old lithography mask sets had two separate pitches between elements. Namely,
a pitch of both 51.37 µm and the desired 51.33 µm. This error was corrected, so there
only was the desired element pitch of 51.33 µm described in section 5.1.2. At the same
time, element numbering was added to all the elements in the 384-element arrays to
make characterisation easier after the fabrication. For all of the arrays and elements,
the probe pads were also changed so they would be easier to probe during the electrical
characterisation.
5.4.2
Addition Of New Test Structures
Two new test arrays were designed in the new design. They were both based on preex-
isting test arrays, but the key difference was that the geometry was varied. These were
designed so it would be possible to determine the pull-in voltage as a function of the
electrode radius and another where the cavity and electrode radii were decreased. Still,
the ratio of the two was constant. This variable radius should allow for the experimental
determination of the plate stiffness and Young’s modulus of the top plate.
Variable Bottom Electrode Radius
For the test structure where the bottom electrode size was varied, it was decreased in 8
steps from the standard size down to no longer being there, and only the interconnects
being present in the cavity. The number of steps meant there would be nine possible
values of the electrode radius to fit the pull-in voltage, which should be adequate as the
pull-in voltage scales with the inverse radii. In section 2.2.1, it was assumed in the deriva-
tion that the total area of the CMUT was part of the capacitor. This assumption may no
longer be valid when the electrode size is decreased. So, to find the effect of the bottom
electrode size on the pull-in voltage, this structure was included. Table 5.4 shows the
different electrode radii. Each electrode radius is present twice in the test array, giving 18
elements in one array.

5.5
Conversion of Mask From Separated Bottom Electrode to
Separated Top Electrode
After finalising the design for the mask design with a common top electrode and imple-
menting all the desired changes and new arrays, the common top electrode design was
converted into a second set of lithography masks with a common bottom electrode. This
change was implemented as the LOCOS based design has a common bottom electrode.
Therefore, to examine the effect of this difference. The second design was needed. The
second set of lithography masks was made so two different designs could be fabricated
and characterised. However, one key difference between the anodic common bottom
electrode design and the LOCOS based design is that the LOCOS based design makes
use of having a silicon substrate, so no interconnects between all the cells had to be
made. Instead, the silicon substrate used is highly doped with phosphorous. [15] [14]
The high doping means the substrate can conduct the current, and all bottom electrodes
can be connected without manually interconnecting the elements. For the anodic design,
the bottom substrate is boron-doped glass. This substrate is an insulator, and the con-
nections between all the bottom electrodes must be etched down into the glass and made
using a layer of gold. This method is similar to the connections between the cells in the
same element in the common top electrode anodic design. Likewise, the top electrode
had to be separated throughout the entire design; changes had to be made to the probe
pads and new openings down to the bottom electrode had to be made. An example of
the difference between the two sets of lithography masks can be seen in fig. 5.3. Here,
the difference between the probe pads and the array can be seen. The large squares are
40
Design, Fabrication and Characterization of a CMUT for a Linear Array Probe
==PAGE:49==
CHAPTER 5. DESIGN
called the probe pads, where contact with the individual electrodes is obtained. The large
pad along the bottom and side connects to the large common electrode. The other key
difference between the two designs is that the horizontal connections between the ele-
ments’ bottom electrodes can be seen in fig. 5.3b. Likewise, the top electrode in fig. 5.3b
is made with hexagons, so only the cells within the same array are connected, but it is
no longer connected to the neighbouring arrays as with the common top electrode design
shown in fig. 5.3a. The comb-like structure of the large bottom pad can also be seen. This
structure was needed to reduce the total area over which the poly-Silicon On Insulators
(PSOIs) were bonded over. If not included, the thinning down of the top plate could have
resulted in the nitride layer cracking and giving pieces of poly-silicon and nitride on the
electrodes and potentially shorting elements.
(a) Probe pads and CMUT cells for the com-
mon top electrode mask set.
(b) Probe pads and CMUT cells for the com-
mon bottom electrode mask set.
Figure 5.3: Comparison of the two sets of lithography masks. On the left is the common
top electrode design, and on the right is the common bottom electrode design. The image
is a cutout from the bottom right corner of the 384 elements array.
The new numbering of the arrays can also be seen in the figure for all elements. The plus
sign at the left of the number 01 in both mask sets is an alignment mark for wire bonding.
It has been included to allow for automatic wire bonding of all elements to the PCB before
encapsulating the array in PDMS for acoustical characterisation.
5.6
Chapter Summary
In this chapter, the specification for the new design was presented along with the dimen-
sions of the CMUT to obtain the required 15 MHz submersion resonance frequency in
water. The simulation models used to find this and the difference between the MATLAB/-
COMSOL model and the COMSOL model used were also presented along with the new
addition of finding the resonance frequency of the CMUTs submerged in water while en-
capsulated in PDMS and other suggestions for improvements of the simulation models.
Lastly, the reason for the two sets of lithography masks was presented and how they
differed.

Wafer map
This chapter aims to provide an overview of the layout of designed lithography masks,
the arrays present in the mask sets, and their location, making it easier to identify which
arrays have been characterised and convey their results without ambiguity. This chapter
will also describe how lithography masks are built into mask sets.
6.1
Overview of Arrays and Hierarchical Structure
The design of a lithography mask set is built up in a hierarchy of separate layers. Each
layer is one mask and defines the geometry of the individual parts of the design. For both
designs made in this thesis, four layers were used. One for defining the etch of the cavities
in the mask, one for defining the bottom electrodes in the etched cavities, one for opening
down through the top plate to access the bottom electrode after bonding and lastly, one for
defining the top electrodes themselves. As described earlier in chapter 4, 384-element
linear arrays have been fabricated before using an anodic process. However, the final
performance of them was not as intended. Some of it could be explained by the errors
made in fabrication, but not all could be explained by this. [16, 17, 18] This work will learn
from the process advancement which has been made since the last batch of transducers
were fabricated and using updated simulation models to fabricate 15 MHz linear 384-
element transducers. Therefore, most of the arrays to be presented are not new, but
small changes have been made to the dimensions since then.
As mentioned in the design chapter, chapter 5, there is more than one array on the wafer,
and an overview of the arrays on the wafer can be seen in fig. 6.1. Where fig. 6.1a shows
the mask layout as it looks in the software L-Edit used to design the lithography mask.
All the masks are drawn as positive masks, which is the simplest way to draw them.
Not all masks are to be used as positive masks, but the exposure tool that will be used
can digitally invert the lithography mask before exposure. Figure 6.1b shows the naming
convention and where the different arrays in the designs are located as this is not as easily
seen from the overview in fig. 6.1a. The naming scheme in fig. 6.1b will be vital for clearly
defining which array is being characterised and for making notes of any defects found
during the in-cleanroom characterisation and subsequent electrical characterisation. In
total, there are five different arrays in the lithography masks. They are distributed into a
grid of 3 rows and 13 columns. The 384-element linear arrays are shown as black-outlined
rectangular boxes. In rows 1 and 3, there are 8 of the 384-element linear arrays and 13
in the second row, giving 29 arrays per wafer. A high number of arrays makes it possible
to determine the yield and see any process variation across a wafer. On top of the 384-
element linear arrays, there are also four different types of test arrays. The simplest test
array is 16 linear elements placed with a pitch of 340 µm. These linear elements have the
same dimensions as the elements from the 384-element linear array. These are shown
as yellow squares in fig. 6.1b. The next array is the same linear elements but placed
in the λ/2 pitch and in 16 groups of 7 linear arrays. These are shown as the light blue
square boxes. The newly designed test array where the radius is varied is shown as the
light green squares, and finally, the test array where the electrode size is varied is shown
as the purple squares. The red squares labelled as sacrificial elements in fig. 6.1b are
identical to the four test arrays. They were added to fill out the last remaining space on
the wafer, and they could also help keep the etch uniformity when etching down through
the poly-silicon top plate. There are also smaller single-element test structures between

without damaging the 384-element linear arrays and, at the same time, having them close
to the 384-element linear arrays. Thus, stability elements on the 65 cell test element will
not affect the 384-element linear arrays. They are numbered based on the other elements:
the first number for the row and the second number for the column. The difference is the

7
Fabrication
This chapter will describe the process of fabricating the designed linear CMUT arrays.
This fabrication process is long, and it has thus been divided into three parts. The first
part will cover the fabrication of the top PSOI wafers used. The second part will cover how
the cavities and bottom electrodes were formed in the bottom glass wafers. The third part
will cover the fabrication process following the bonding until the fabrication is finished.
The challenges encountered during the fabrication will also be presented and discussed.
The complete process flow can be found in appendix A.
7.1
Overview of Fabrication Process
Figure 7.1: Fabrication process for the top PSOI wafers by a series of furnace processes
and a polishing step. The sketch is not to scale. The silicon wafer is 350 µm thick, and
the oxy-nitride layer is around 200 nm thick.
Two sets of wafers are needed for the fabrication. The first wafer is a PSOI wafer. An
overview of the fabrication of it is given in fig. 7.1. The process of fabricating them is
a series of furnace processes and polishing. It starts with a blank silicon wafer, step 1.
Then a Buried Oxide Layer (BOX) layer is grown on both sides of the wafer, step 2. Then
poly-silicon is deposited by LPCVD on both sides of the wafer, step 3. Then, one of the
sides of the wafer is polished by Chemical Mechanical Polishing (CMP) to give a smooth
surface, step 4. Lastly, a layer of oxy-nitride is deposited by LPCVD and oxidation. These
wafers are unstructured and consist of layers of different thin films.
The second set of wafers used is borofloat glass wafers, which are structured to have the
bottom electrodes in cavities. The PSOI is then bonded to the borofloat wafer, and the
top layers and handle silicon wafer thinned down to the polished poly-silicon layer, and
the top electrodes are defined in the polished poly-silicon layer. The fabrication process
is shown in fig. 7.2. The fabrication process starts with a blank borofloat wafer, step 1.
Then Cr is deposited and etched selectively using the first lithography mask to form a
hard mask, step 2. Then the cavities are etched down into the borofloat substrate, step 3.
Then, an adhesion layer and gold (Au) is deposited on the wafers, step 5. Then, the Au
is selectively etched using the second lithography mask to form the bottom electrodes,
step 5. The PSOI is bonded to the borofloat wafer by anodic bonding, step 6. The PSOI
is then thinned down to the polished poly-silicon layer by a series of etches, step 7. The
opening down to the bottom electrodes is then made using the third lithography mask,
48
Design, Fabrication and Characterization of a CMUT for a Linear Array Probe
==PAGE:57==
CHAPTER 7. FABRICATION
Figure 7.2: Fabrication process for the bottom wafer and bonding process with the top
PSOI included. The process requires the use of four lithography masks. One for defining
the Chromium (Cr) etch mask at step 2, a second for defining the bottom electrodes at
step 5, a third at step 8 to open down to the bottom electrode and a last at step 9 to define
the top electrode.
step 8. Lastly, the Al for the top electrode is deposited, and the top electrodes are defined
using the fourth lithography mask, step 9.
7.2
Fabrication of PSOIs
This section will cover the fabrication of wafers used for the top plate in the CMUTs. These
are called poly-Silicon On Insulators (PSOIs) as they are silicon wafers on which an insu-
lating silicon oxide is grown, followed by the deposition of a layer of poly-silicon and then
polished to give a flat surface with nano-scale surface roughness, a layer of oxynitride
is deposited to serve as an insulating layer between the top and bottom electrodes. The
PSOIs were the first wafers to be fabricated as getting the right poly-silicon thickness can
be difficult, and by fabricating the PSOIs first, the radius in the design could be adjusted
if the thickness of the poly-silicon was not as intended.
7.2.1
Wafer Selection and Wafer Cleaning
For this, a silicon substrate is used as it can be oxidised to form the insulating oxide and
can go into the equipment to deposit the poly-silicon and oxynitride. 25 Double Side Pol-
ished (DSP) (100) phosphorous-doped silicon wafers were selected for this. They were
chosen as they are the thinnest possible to get at DTU Nanolab, which will decrease the
etch time significantly when thinning down the top plate after the bonding process. The
wafers should be clean as they came from a new sealed box. Nevertheless, they were
cleaned before the oxidation to make the BOX layer to prevent any particles or contami-
nants from getting buried in the film and potentially causing problems with film uniformity.
The cleaning process used was the RCA process, a multi-step process consisting of 3
chemical baths and water rinses. The chemical baths are RCA 1 consisting of NH4OH,
H2O2 and H2O (DI-water) in ratio 1:1:5 heated to 70 °C, RCA 2 consisting of Hydrochloric
acid (HCL), H2O2 and H2O (DI-water) in a ratio of 1:1:5 heated to 70 °C and a 1% HF
solution. This process will remove most metallic and organic contaminants on the wafers.
[45] Following immediately after the RCA cleaning of the wafers, they were oxidised in
a dry oxidation process in a horizontal oxidisation furnace (manufactured by Tempress)
for 12 hours and 30 minutes at 1100 °C where an oxygen flow of 5 SLM was constantly
pumped into the furnace and flowed over there wafers. The targeted thickness was 500
Design, Fabrication and Characterization of a CMUT for a Linear Array Probe
49
==PAGE:58==
CHAPTER 7. FABRICATION
nm, and afterwards, 513 nm of oxide was measured using reflectometry on a Filmtek
4000 Spectrometer manufactured by Scientific Computing International. The same spec-
trometer was used for all the subsequent film thicknesses as well. The wafer used to
measure the oxide thickness had to be left behind as a control wafer per DTU Nanolab
requirements after using the furnace to oxidise the PSOIs. Thus, 24 PSOIs was left for
further processing.
7.2.2
Deposition of Poly silicon
Following the oxide deposition, the wafers were taken directly to a LPCVD furnace for
depositing the poly-silicon. The target thickness is 4.1 µm, meaning the deposition had
to be done in several depositions. A total of four depositions were needed. A horizontal
LPCVD furnace from Tempress was used, dedicated to only deposit poly-silicon. A test
wafer with 296 nm of oxide was placed in the middle of the furnace and used to measure
the thickness of the deposited poly-silicon after each deposition. After each deposition,
it was changed with a new test wafer. The deposition rate was then calculated and used
to estimate the deposition time for the subsequent deposition. An overview of the thick-
ness of the deposited film after each deposition and the deposition time can be found
in table 7.1. For all depositions of poly-silicon, the only process parameter that varied
was the deposition time. The remaining process parameters can be seen in table 7.2.
After the first three depositions, the cumulative thickness was 4068 nm according to the
dummy wafers, close to the intended thickness of poly-silicon. However, after unload-
ing the PSOIs from the furnace, the thickness was measured on PSOI 14 and 25 using
reflectometry. Here, it was found that in the middle of the box, the thickness was 3799
nm, and at the edge, it was only 3401 nm, far from the intended 4100 nm. Reflectometry
can be inaccurate when measuring films that are several micrometres thick. Therefore,
both of the PSOIs were cleaved, and the thickness of the poly-silicon was measured in
a Scanning Electron Microscope (SEM) by examining the cross-section. The thickness
was measured to be 3746 nm and 3425 nm for PSOI 14 and 25, respectively. A complete
overview of all the thicknesses measured is given in table 7.4 at the end of the section.
This difference across a box of PSOIs has been described earlier in the MEMS applied
sensors group.[46] However, the overall thickness was far from the targeted 4100 nm.
Therefore, the remaining wafers were given an RCA clean again to remove any con-
taminants from the wafers. Then, the wafers were loaded in the LPCVD furnace again,
and a fourth deposition of poly-silicon was performed. After the deposition, 352 nm of
poly-silicon was measured on the test wafer used to monitor thickness. This thickness
was verified with reflectometry on the central PSOI 12, where 4122 nm of poly was mea-
sured, and for PSOI 24 at the edge, 3993 nm was measured. After cleaving PSOI 24
and measuring the cross-section in a SEM, the thickness was found to be 3874 nm. This
thickness is still lower than anticipated, but the difference from the middle to the edge of
a box can be up to 10 %, and the PSOI 12 from the middle was measured to be 4122 nm
with reflectometry.[46] Indicating that the thickness in the middle of the boat was at the
desired thickness, and the PSOIs could be polished. After the poly-silicon deposition and
the cleaving of two PSOIs, 21 PSOIs were remaining.
Following the poly-silicon deposition, 14 of the wafers were polished using Chemical Me-
chanical Polishing (CMP), where an abrasive slurry combined with a polishing pad re-
moves part of the surface of the wafer when it is pushed down onto the polishing pad. At
the same time, it is being rotated along with the polishing pad rotating and the chuck with
the wafer moving in and out on the polishing pad. This process leaves a uniform and flat
surface. While polishing PSOI 21, it was noted that the machine had run out of slurry.
The polished PSOI was the last PSOI to be polished, and it was only in the last part of the

7.2.4
Oxynitride Deposition
With 14 polished PSOIs. The last step was to deposit the final insulating layer of oxy-
nitride to prevent shortcircuiting of the CMUTs. The 14 polished wafers were RCA cleaned
again and taken to a horizontal furnace manufactured by Tempress to be oxidised for 15
minutes at 1000 °C in a dry oxidisation and annealed for 20 minutes. 22.5 nm of oxide
was measured on a test wafer after the oxidation using reflectometry. Then, the PSOIs
were taken directly to a horizontal LPCVD furnace manufactured by Tempress, which was
dedicated to nitride deposition and had nitride deposited for 1 hour. After the deposition,
197 nm of stoichiometric silicon nitride was measured on an included test wafer using
reflectometry. The process parameters for the nitride deposition are stated in table 7.3.
Following the nitride deposition, the PSOIs were again taken to the horizontal oxidation
furnace and oxidised for 3 hours at 1100 °C in a wet oxidisation. After the oxidation,
a 1065 nm thick oxide was measured on a test wafer using reflectometry. During the
nitride deposition, a fault on a gas line for the nitride furnace meant the deposition had
not started initially, and DTU nanolab staff had to change a gas line. Initially, this was
not considered a problem, and processing of the PSOIs continued. However, after the
wet oxidation, the severity of the gas line change became apparent when scanning the
wafers for particles as a last check before any bonding was performed. The PSOIs were
covered in particles which could not be seen with the bare eye. For most of the PSOIs, the
particle counts were over 200,000 per wafer. The exception was PSOI 06, where 56.310
particles were measured. However, this was still far from being within acceptable values
and would likely never have bonded successfully. A particle scan from PSOI 12 can be
seen in fig. 7.6 and compared with the particle scan in fig. 7.4a which is also from PSOI
12, the drastic increase in the particle count can be seen. The particle count was so big
the particle scanner could not locate the major flat of the wafer.

Before proceeding, it was investigated if
the nitride furnace indeed was the source
of the particles as suspected, and the two
test wafers included in the oxidations right
before and after the nitride deposition were
scanned for particles, along with the test
wafer from the nitride furnace and the test
wafer from the previous user of the ni-
tride furnace. The particle scans from the
four different test wafers can be seen in
fig. 7.7. These particle scans clearly show
that it was during the nitride deposition on
the PSOIs that the nitride furnace malfunc-
tioned, as all other test wafers had low par-
ticle numbers within expected values from
standard cleanroom processing when care
is not taken to minimise particles. On the
other hand, the test wafer from the nitride
deposition on the PSOIs had particle counts in the same range as all the PSOIs. There-
fore, the particles must have come from the LPCVD nitride furnace. The Thinfilm group,
which is responsible for the furnaces at DTU Nanolab, confirmed this in a subsequent
nitride deposition performed on dummy wafers. Removing the particle-filled film would
take some time, so it was first attempted to see if cleaning the PSOIs with the RCA 1
solution for 10 minutes could remove the particles. In the RCA 1 solution, a newly bought
Design, Fabrication and Characterization of a CMUT for a Linear Array Probe
53
==PAGE:62==
CHAPTER 7. FABRICATION
wafer was included, as well as a control for the particle count from the RCA 1 bath. It
was scanned for particles right before and after the cleaning. Unfortunately, the number
of particles on the PSOIs did not decrease, which can be seen from the particle scan of
PSOI 12 after the RCA 1 cleaning in fig. 7.5, and comparing the particle count from be-
fore the RCA 1 clean, fig. 7.6. This result indicates that the particles were embedded in
the oxy-nitride film itself, and it would likely be impossible to remove them through any
cleaning processes. The particle count had increased for the newly bought control wafer
but was still around 700 particles, the majority of which were below 200 nm in size, which
is smaller than the HEPA filters at DTU Nanolab can filter out. Based on this, it was de-
cided that the best way to remove the particle would be by removing the oxy-nitride film
and re-depositing it after DTU Nanolab staff resolved the particle issue with the nitride
furnace.

the PSOIs, which has the entire surface covered in particles. These scans indicate that
the nitride deposition on the PSOIs generated the particles, in agreement with the theory
presented in section 2.5.3.
Removal of Particle-Filled Nitride Film
To remove the oxy-nitride, the PSOIs were etched in BHF, then in hot phosphoric acid
and then again in BHF. Hot phosphoric acid is usually used to etch nitride, as the film
54
Design, Fabrication and Characterization of a CMUT for a Linear Array Probe
==PAGE:63==
CHAPTER 7. FABRICATION
is an oxy-nitride, the hot phosphoric acid will not be able to etch the outermost layer of
oxygen-rich nitride. Therefore, the initial BHF etch was needed as it will etch away the
oxygen-rich nitride. The PSOIs were etched for 10 minutes in the BHF, rinsed in water
and then for 1 hour and 10 minutes in 160 °C hot phosphoric acid and then again etched
for 10 minutes in BHF to remove the oxide below the nitride. After the etching, some
traces of nitride were still at the edges of the PSOIs. Camera images of the marks can
be seen in fig. 7.8. This surface roughness can cause problems later during bonding,
and all the oxy-nitride must be removed. Therefore, the oxy-nitride etching procedure
was repeated. After performing the etching for the second time, all of the oxy-nitride was
successfully etched away. However, after the etching of the oxy-nitride, it was noticed
that the PSOIs had been put back in the box with the front facing the handle side and
accidentally been handled on the front side with vacuum tweezers. The PSOIs were put
back correctly in the wafer box. However, PSOI 12 and 21 had been placed with the front
down and photographed. The camera photos in fig. 7.8 also show a matte surface from
the unpolished side having a matte surface from the higher surface roughness from the
non-polished side. Consequently, these two PSOIs should preferably be used only for
future cross-sectional SEM imaging as the surface may have been scratched, leading to
a potentially higher chance of bad results during bonding. The scratching could have been
verified by scanning the surface with an AFM or entirely removed by polishing them again.
However, this would have been time-consuming, and some PSOIs would be needed for
future cross-sectional measurements after all, and therefore, it was not done. After the
etching using the phosphoric acid, the PSOIs had to be cleaned as the bath can add traces
of Potassium hydroxide (KOH). The potential traces were removed by cleaning the PSOIs
for 10 minutes in a chemical bath containing 80 °warm sulphuric acid where a spoonful of
ammonium persulfate is added to start a chemical reaction similar to that of the piranha
solution. The cleaning left the PSOIs as before the oxynitride was deposited and as clean
as possible for depositing a new layer of oxy-nitride.

7.3
Fabrication of Cavities and Bottom Electrodes
This section will cover the fabrication of the bottom glass wafers from the start with the
wafer selection, the cavity etching and bottom electrode formation until the bonding to the
top PSOI wafers. This part of the fabrication process corresponds to steps 1 through 5 in
fig. 7.2.
7.3.1
Selection of Wafers and Etching of Cavities
To fabricate the bottom wafers. 12 4-inch borofloat-33 wafers were selected. They all
had a thickness of 500 µm and were double-sided polished. After choosing the wafers,
they were taken for the deposition of the Cr used as a hard mask to etch the cavities.
The wafers were not cleaned before the deposition; they should have been as clean as
possible after the selection. The deposition of Cr was done by electron beam (e-beam)
deposition using a temescal manufactured by ferrotec. Which locally heats the metal to
be deposited using an electron beam, which will then condense on the surface of the
wafer. This process is performed in high vacuum with a pressure around 10−6 Torr (0.133
mPa) to reduce particle contamination in the film. 50 nm of Cr was deposited on the 12
wafers with a deposition rate of 5 Å/s. The deposition was performed in three batches,
as only four wafers could be deposited simultaneously. e-beam evaporation is preferred
over thermal evaporation as the deposited film will have fewer contaminants. As the de-
posited Cr was a uniform film layer, to define the etch mask, 2 µm of positive AZ MiR 701
(MiR) photoresist was spin-coated on top of it on all the wafers. The wafers were also
given an Hexamethyldisilazane (HMDS) pre-bake was included to improve the adhesion
of the photoresist to the Cr layer. The HMDS pre-bake was performed at 90 °C. A Gamma
2M spin coater manufactured by Süss microtec was used to perform the pre-baking and
spin-coating. This machine is an automatic spin-coater, increasing reproducibility and uni-
formity across wafers for more consistent results. During the exposure, the wafers were
split up as two separate sets of lithography masks had been designed. Wafers 01-06
were exposed with the common top electrode design, and wafers 07-12 were exposed
with the common bottom electrode design. All wafers were then exposed with the first
mask, the cavity mask, in the respective set of lithography masks. To perform the ex-
posure a MLA150 manufactured by Heidelberg Instruments was used. This exposure
system is a maskless aligner (MLA), meaning it does not rely on a physical mask like a
conventional one. Instead, it exposes the digital mask file directly on a wafer. For all 12
wafers, an exposure dose of 320 m J/cm2 was used along with a defocus value1 of 0.
All exposures were performed using the quality mode for the highest possible resolution.
The wafers’ photoresist was developed by a post-exposure bake (PEB) at 110 °C for 60
s and then developed for 60 s with Tetramethylammonium hydroxide (TMAH) in a puddle
development. Lastly, the TMAH was rinsed off using water. The development was per-
formed using a Gamma 2M developer manufactured by Süss MicroTec. When the first
wafer of each of the designs had been exposed, it was developed while the exposure of
1The defocus value is a value which will shift the focal plane of the optical system up and down compared
to the autofocus of the system and is determined in the same way as the correct exposure dose.
Design, Fabrication and Characterization of a CMUT for a Linear Array Probe
57
==PAGE:66==
CHAPTER 7. FABRICATION
the second wafer was started. Thus, the exposed pattern could be checked and verified
to see if the correct mask had been used. It was done using an optical microscope, and an
example of the exposed pattern from wafer 01 can be seen in fig. 7.11 where the exposed
and developed cavity photoresist mask radius is 21.0 µm, as the designed value is in the
lithography mask file.

of wafer 01 after exposure and develop-

right dimensions.
To transfer the pattern of the photoresist down
into the Cr and form the hard mask. All wafers
were etched using Cr etch 18 for 40 seconds,
after which the pattern appeared fully etched
when looking at the wafers after lifting them out
of the beaker as the locations of the arrays had
become transparent.
When the wafers were
examined in an optical microscope to verify the
pattern had been transferred down into the Cr.
It was noticed that it was not on the entire wafer.
The pattern was fully etched into the Cr. An

strate, the cavities could be etched using BHF. The expected etch rate of BHF in borofloat
is around 24nm/min. Therefore, to obtain the desired vacuum gap and electrode thick-
ness, a cavity depth of 350 nm was targeted, meaning the wafers had to be etched for
14 minutes and 30 seconds. All wafers were etched for this time using a BHF without
a wetting agent to minimise under-etching of the Cr mask. After the etching of the bo-
rofloat, the photoresist was removed by submerging the wafers in NMP Remover 1165
with ultrasonic agitation for 15 minutes, then in isopropyl alcohol for 5 minutes, also with
ultrasound agitation, and lastly, rinsed in water to remove any residues of the chemicals.
Before the remaining Cr was removed, the etched cavity depth was verified by measuring
the depths in the centre and at the south of wafers 01, 06, 07 and 12. The measurements
were performed using stylos profilometry on a DektakXTA manufactured by Bruker. A
depth of around 430 nm was measured on all wafers. The Cr was still on the surface, the
measured depth would be around 50 nm higher than the actual cavity depth. Still, this
places the actual depth higher than the projected depth from the etch time and etch rate,
but this can be compensated by depositing more metal for the bottom electrodes, and the
Cr could be removed. The Crwas then removed by etching it in Cr etch 18 for 2 minutes,
and afterwards, small Cr spots were left where the wafers had rested on the wafer holder
used in the etch. Despite this, the cavity depth was measured in five locations on all 12
wafers, and the measured cavity depths can be seen in table 7.5. After the cavity mea-
surement, the wafers were cleaned in 80 °C warm sulphuric acid mixed with a spoonful of
ammonium persulfate for 10 minutes, followed by a 20-minute clean using piranha, due
to the Cr still being left on the some of the wafers. All of them were etched in Cr etch 18
for 5 minutes to ensure all Cr were removed. The wafer holder was also turned for a more
even etch. Afterwards, the cavities were measured again, and the measured depths can
be seen in table 7.6. The second measurement was performed to verify the depth was
the same in the case small residues of Cr had been left before. The measured depth was
not entirely the same, meaning there might have been small amounts of Cr left on the
wafers, which could not have been seen.

7.3.2
Deposition of Metal for Bottom Electrodes
To deposit the metal for the bottom electrodes, the average cavity depth from table 7.6
was used to calculate the amount of metal to be deposited to obtain a gap height of 85 nm.
From table 7.6, it can be seen that the average cavity depth is 382.6 nm. Therefore, to
obtain a gap height of 85 nm, a total of 297 nm of metal for the bottom electrodes needed
to be deposited. Before the deposition, the wafers were cleaned again using hot sulphuric
acid mixed with a spoonful of ammonium persulfate and followed by a 10-minute piranha
mixed in the ratio of 4:1 to ensure any last contaminants on the wafer surface had been

For all four depositions, a deposition rate of 1 Å/s was used to minimise the condensation
of the metals in drops, which can be a problem with Au. Likewise, for all of the deposi-
tions of the bottom electrodes, the e-beam evaporation was not vented to atmospheric
pressure between the deposition of the adhesion layer and the Au layer. After the deposi-
tions, the wafers are checked for Au particles. The check was performed using an optical
microscope in dark-field where any scattered light from the particles will be unmistakable
on the dark background. The amount of Au particles was low. However, some larger
particles were found. An example can be seen in fig. 7.13 where the difference between
bright-field and dark-field microscopy and how particles light up in dark-field can be seen.
Since the amount of Au particles was small, the processing continued.
7.3.3
Lithography - Defining the Bottom Electrodes
To define an etch mask to etch and define the bottom electrodes inside the cavities. All 12
wafers and the 4 dummy wafers were pre-baked with HMDS and then spin-coated with a
1.5 µm thick layer of AZ 5214E (5214E) photoresist. The 5214E photoresist is a negative
tone photoresist. However, the tone of the photoresist can be reversed after exposure
before it is developed. In this case, the image reversal will be performed as the photoresist
must protect the metal inside the cavities from etching. If a positive tone photoresist was
used and everything but the intended bottom electrodes were exposed, the metal-covered
60
Design, Fabrication and Characterization of a CMUT for a Linear Array Probe
==PAGE:69==
CHAPTER 7. FABRICATION
(a) Dark-field image of a Au particle.
(b) Bright-field image of a Au particle.
Figure 7.13: Comparison of dark-field and bright-field microscope images of the same Au
particle. The only difference between the two images was whether bright-field or dark-
field was used and if the illumination was changed to get a proper sample illumination.
sidewalls of the cavities could reflect the UV light used in the exposure and expose the
parts of the photoresist that should not be exposed. The image reversal will add some
extra processing. However, the pattern will be more well-defined. Therefore, the 5214E
was used. The wafers were then exposed with the same MLA following the spin-coating
with an exposure dose of 80 mJ/c2, and a defocus value of -2 was used for all the exposed
wafers. The exception to this was the first exposed wafers, which was one of the Ti/Au
dummy wafers, where an exposure dose of 75 mJ/cm2 and a defocus of 0 was used by
accident. Since it was a dummy wafer intended to time the etch rate, it was not crucial
and worth the added time to remove, re-spin-coat the wafer and expose the photoresist
again. Thus, the processing continued with other wafers. Following the exposures, the
wafers were baked at 110 °C for 120 seconds, then flood exposed with a dose of 198
mJ/cm2 and lastly developed for 60 seconds with TMAH in a puddle development. As
in the exposure of the first lithography mask. The first exposed wafer with each design
was developed and checked in an optical microscope to verify that the dimensions of the
pattern were correct and that the resist was in the cavities. An example from wafer 01
can be seen in fig. 7.14 where the pattern is fully developed, has the correct dimensions
and is aligned inside the cavities. Thus, the wafers were ready for etching of the bottom
electrodes.
7.3.4
Etching of Bottom Electrodes
To etch the bottom electrodes, IBE had to be used since it is the only method for dry
etching of Au at DTU Nanolab. As described in section 2.5.2, the strength of IBE is also a
major downside. If the etch time used is not carefully controlled, the substrate underneath
will also be etched. Therefore, the test wafers included in the Au deposition and previous
lithography step were etched first to time the etch rate and determine how long the wafers
should be etched. The system used for the etching was an IBE/IBSD Ionfab 300 manu-
factured by Oxford Instruments Plasma Technology, where the recipe kits Au acceptance
-the angle was used. This recipe has been developed for etching Au with a lower etch
rate and minimising resist burning. The process parameters are given in table 7.8. By
performing the etch on the test wafers in smaller steps, the etch rate time was timed to be
4 minutes and 50 seconds for the stack of Ta/Au and 5 minutes for the Ta/Au stack using
Design, Fabrication and Characterization of a CMUT for a Linear Array Probe
61
==PAGE:70==
CHAPTER 7. FABRICATION
the dummy wafers. To check if the etch was done, the wafer could be held up against the
light, and it could be seen when the wafers turned transparent after the metal was etched
away. With these etch times, the 12 structured wafers were then etched, and afterwards,
the pattern was checked in an optical microscope to verify the etch had gone well and
that all metal had been removed. An example can be seen from the bottom electrode on
wafer 10 in fig. 7.15 where the metal can be seen to have been etched away.
