After the etching of the bottom electrodes, it was decided to only continue with four wafers
to reduce the processing time at each process step. To still have each combination of
metals and designs represented, it was decided to continue with wafers 02, 04, 08 and
10. The reason for it being these wafers from each of the possible combinations is that
they were the wafers from each combination which had the final vacuum gap closest to the
desired 85 nm as per table 7.6 when 297 of metal for the bottom electrode was deposited.
Those four wafers were then etched in Transene GE-8110 for 30 seconds while stirring
to remove any re-deposited Au on the photoresist. The etch is short as the chemical will
also etch the Au bottom electrodes if the etch is performed for too long. After removing
the photoresist, the re-deposited metal has been seen earlier to shortcircuit neighbouring
elements. [18] At this point in the processing, an APV also had to be made to work with
the chemical as the one previously made for it could not be found, and the chemical is not
a standard chemical at DTU nanolab. The APV made is included in appendix C.
The removal of the photoresist after the re-deposited Au had been etched away was split
into two separate steps. The first was a short and mild plasma ashing for 15 minutes
with an oxygen flow of 400 mL/min and a nitrogen flow of 70 mL/min with a power of
500 W using a TePla model 300 Plasma Processor. The plasma ashing was followed
62
Design, Fabrication and Characterization of a CMUT for a Linear Array Probe
==PAGE:71==
CHAPTER 7. FABRICATION
by a 15-minute wet resist stripping using ultrasonic agitation in NMP Remover 1165, 5
minutes with ultrasonic agitation in isopropanol and then a water rinse. Some resist was
still left on the electrodes, so a second wet resist strip was performed. This time, only for
10 minutes with ultrasonic agitation in NMP Remover 1165, followed by 5 minutes with
ultrasonic agitation in isopropanol and then a water rinse at the end. Some specks of
photoresist were still present after the second wet resist strip. However, removing these
with the NMP Remover 1165 could take quite a long time. Therefore, it was decided to
leave the small photoresist residues as the wafers had to be cleaned before bonding with
a piranha solution. The piranha solution should be more than capable of removing the last
traces of organic photoresist. Therefore, processing the four wafers continued despite the
small photoresist residues. An example of the small amounts of photoresist observed can
be seen in an image taken with an optical microscope of wafer 02 in fig. 7.16. In the same
image, the corners of the contact pads can also be seen to have been rounded after the
re-deposited Au was etched.
Figure 7.14: Optical microscope image
to verify correct exposure and alignment
of the exposed photoresist to the cavi-
ties on wafer 01.
The height profiles of the four wafers were mea-
sured to verify the cavity depth before the bond-
ing. This measurement will give the final gap
height, and the pull-in voltage is different from
the designed value during the electrical charac-
terisation. The gap height was measured using
stylus profilometry, and the results of the mea-
surements are stated in table 7.9 along with ini-
tial cavity depth, the projected gap height and
the difference between the two projected and
measured gap heights. From this table, it can
be seen that the overall gap height was found
to be larger than the projected gap height, with
a few exceptions. Therefore, some of the Au
would appear to have fallen off during the re-
sist stripping process. It will most likely have
been during the wet-resist stripping process.
This behaviour is called cavitation, where the
ultrasonic agitation removes some metal and
the photoresist. The problem of this phenomenon is its randomness, which is also ap-
parent from table 7.9 where the difference varies wildly from wafer to wafer and across
the same wafer. The photoresist being left on some parts of the bottom electrode would
not explain this effect, as this would lower the measured gap height. The differences in
the final gap height mean the pull-in voltage will also vary. Despite the occurrence of this
effect, the processing of the wafers was ready to be bonded to the top PSOIs. Since the
gap heights are known, the cavitation effect could explain any considerable wafer-scale
variation in the experimentally measured pull-in voltage.
7.4
Wafer Bonding and Fabrication of Top Electrodes
This section will cover the part of the fabrication from the bonding of the bottom borofloat
wafers to the end of the fabrication inside the cleanroom, corresponding to steps 6 to 9 in
fig. 7.2.
7.4.1
Wafer Cleaning and Bonding
Before the bonding, the bottom wafers and the PSOIs had to be cleaned to remove any
possible particles or contaminants present on the surface of the wafers. Two different
Design, Fabrication and Characterization of a CMUT for a Linear Array Probe
63
==PAGE:72==
CHAPTER 7. FABRICATION
Figure 7.16: Microscope image of wafer 02 showing the small amounts of resist left. The
resist is the speck at the left contact pad in the top right corner. The corners are also more
rounded due to the etching of the re-deposited Au.
methods were used to clean the wafers and the PSOIs. The PSOIs were cleaned using
the RCA 1 solution for 10 minutes while the borofloat wafers were cleaned in piranha.
Due to the risk of the piranha under etching the Ti adhesion layer, the wafers which had Ti
as the adhesion layer were cleaned for 2 minutes in piranha and the wafer with Ta as the
adhesion layer were cleaned for 5 minutes in piranha. The different cleaning procedures
are because the borofloat wafers are not allowed in the batch for the RCA 1 solution as it is
reserved only for silicon wafers. This cleaning of the wafers was done early in the morning
to avoid particles in the cleanroom, as the particle count is lower in the mornings due to
the air having been replaced several times throughout the night. For every replacement,
the air is filtered through HEPA filters. As an extra precaution, a face mask was also worn
to prevent particles from falling from the breath and ending up on the wafers after they
were cleaned.
Before the cleaning, the chemical baths, beakers, water rinse baths, etc., were rinsed
thoroughly to remove any particles left from previous users, as these could end up on the
wafers and result in voids. Afterwards, the PSOIs were spin-dried in a spin drier dedicated
for RCA cleaned wafers and transferred to a closed box afterwards. The borofloat was, on
the other hand, all blow-dried using a nitrogen gun as the spin drier, which could be used
to dry them, is used for all processes and likely would contaminate the wafers. Afterwards,
and onto the bonding, new gloves were put on every time the wafers were handled, and
they were replaced every time something other than the wafers was handled. The gloves
were also replaced between each hand bond of the wafers and PSOIs. It was performed
under HEPA filters where the major flats of the two wafers were aligned, and then the
entire surface was put in contact. The initial contact of the wafers may have left some
trapped air bubbles in between the two wafers. Therefore, pressure was applied by hand
from the middle of the wafers and going outwards, so any stuck air between them was
pushed out. This procedure was performed for all of the four wafers.
After cleaning and initial hand bonding, the wafers were taken to be anodically bonded to
form the final bond. The bonding was done in a vacuum chamber where after the wafer
stack was transferred into it was pumped down to 5·10−3 mbar and then heated to 350 °C.
For the bonding, a central electrode is first placed in contact with the wafer stack, and a
bias of 50 V is applied across the wafer stack for an initial weak bond. Then, an electrode

height after the final resist strip. It is compared to the projected gap height, which is the
measured cavity depth after removing the Cr mask, plus the thickness of the deposited
metal for the bottom electrode. It can be seen that, generally, the measured gap height

covering the entire wafer surface is lowered, and a force of 1 bar is applied to the wafer
stack while the bias is increased to 150 V by ramping it with 1 V/s. The bias of 150 V
was held for 10 minutes. This process is a single wafer process, and it was first done for
wafer 10, then wafer 08, followed by wafer 04 and lastly, wafer 02. A graphite sheet was
placed between the PSOIs and the chuck for all the bonds, except the bonding of wafer 10,
to minimise the risk of super-fast etching holes appearing during the KOH etching. The
graphite sheet was not included for wafer 10 as the graphite sheet could not be found
initially. The fast etching holes will be etched faster than the rest of the silicon during the
handle wafer etching and can destroy the entire top plate, so attention should be paid to
the wafers during the etching of the handle layer. Following the bonding process, some of
the wafers had voids, while others had no voids at all. For wafer 04, there were no voids
after the bonding; wafers 10 and 08 had some small voids and wafer 02 had a large void
in the middle of the wafer. For wafer 10, the locations of the voids corresponded to where
particles had been found during the particle scan on PSOI 11, to which it was bonded.
The particle scan is shown in fig. 7.17 along with a camera photograph of wafer 10 after
the bonding, where the voids can be seen through the bottom of the borofloat wafer.
As wafer 02 had the void right in the middle, it was bonded a second time by loading it
into the bonder again and then repeating the bonding procedure described earlier. The
attempt was made since the void right in the middle would be problematic during the
thinning down of the top plate, as the entire top plate may fall off in the last process
steps, and subsequent spin coating may also be difficult with large topography variations.
Unfortunately, the voids did not decrease in size following the second bond. However, the
bonder did report that more chargers had been pulled out of the wafer stack. Indicating a
stronger bond had been obtained. A camera photo was taken before and after the second
bonding, and the difference in the size of the void can be seen in fig. 7.18. Since the void
did not decrease in size, it would indicate the air in the void was already permanently
trapped, and the only way to get it out would be by making a hole down through the top

7.4.2
Thinning Down of Top Plate
The top plate was thinned down in several etches. The etching was done using a com-
bination of dry plasma etching and wet etching with KOH and BHF. The exact process
depended on the material to be etched, which will be described here.
Dry Etching Down To Handle Silicon Wafer
The first part of thinning down the top plate was the removal of the top oxy-nitride, poly-
silicon and BOX layers to reach the handle silicon wafer. These etches were performed
by dry etching using an M/PLEX ICP ASE manufactured by STPS. All borofloat wafers
had to be crystal bonded to a carrier Single Side Polished (SSP) silicon wafer as the
machine can have issued detecting and clamping glass substrates. For etching the oxy-
66
Design, Fabrication and Characterization of a CMUT for a Linear Array Probe
==PAGE:75==
CHAPTER 7. FABRICATION
nitride, the wafers were etched for 3 minutes and 20 seconds using the recipe KITOXYSI,
which will etch the oxygen-rich oxy-nitride. After the etch, the surface of all the wafers was
grey from the exposed poly-silicon underneath the oxy-nitride; thus, all the oxy-nitride was
gone. The recipe shallolr intended for etching shallow trenches in silicon through a bosch
process was then used to etch the poly-silicon. A total of eight cycles of this recipe was
used, after which the poly-silicon could no longer be seen on the surface of the wafers.
Instead, the surface was purple from the exposed BOX layer. The recipe CF4Inductively
Coupled Plasma (ICP) was used to etch the final layer. Initially, wafer 10 was etched for 10
minutes, but a purple ring of BOX could still be seen at the edge from the concentric etch
profile of the system. Therefore, wafer 10 was etched for another 3 minutes to remove all
oxide on the surface entirely. Any oxide present would act as an etch mask for etching
away the silicon handle wafer later. After the additional 3 minutes, the entire surface was
grey from the exposed silicon. All gas flows and recipe parameters for the recipes used
on the ICP system are stated in table 7.10. Note that for the shallolr recipe, all parameters
have two values as it is a bosch process with a passivisation and an etch step per process
cycle.
After exposing the silicon handle wafer, all the wafers were de-crystal bonded from the
carrier wafer, and the backside of the wafers was rinsed using a water gun to remove
any remaining crystal bond on the backside.
The water was then removed from the
backsides using a nitrogen gun. Before removing the silicon handle wafer, the bottom
electrodes were imaged using a microscope through the backside of the wafers, where a
large composite mosaic image was taken showing several of the 384-element linear ar-
rays. These images make it easier to see if there are any large-scale defects, as they can
be missed during normal inspections using an optical microscope. An example can be
seen in fig. 7.19. The images’ vertical lines are from the stitching of the individual images
to form the combined mosaic and are thus not a defect in the fabrication.

Wet Etching of Silicon Handle Wafer
After removing the top layers, the handle wafer was now exposed. It is a 350 µm thick
silicon wafer. Therefore, the wafers were etched in a heated KOH bath, allowing for
etching all the wafers simultaneously. The bath contained a 28 % w/w KOH solution,
which was heated to 90 °C. After the temperature had stabilised, the four wafers were
transferred into the bath and etched for 1 hour and 58 minutes. After which, the wafers
were removed and rinsed in a water bath while the temperature was lowered to 80 °C to
reduce the etch rate to prevent overetching at the end. The wafers were then etched for
1 hour and 55 minutes, after which the silicon had been etched away on wafer 04. Silicon
was still left on most of the surface for the other wafers. However, to prevent the etching
of the KOH from under etching and removing the entire top plate from the wafers, the etch
was stopped, and the wafers rinsed. The wafers were checked regularly to ensure the
super-fast etching holes did not appear on any wafers, especially on wafers 10. However,
they did not appear on any of the wafers. Camera photos of all the wafers can be seen
in fig. 7.20, and in fig. 7.20b, it can be seen that most of the silicon had been removed,

Dry Etching of Silicon Handle Layer Wafer
To etch the remaining silicon of the handle wafers on the wafers, the same ICP system
used to etch the top layers was used again. This time, the recipe kitiso was used. This
recipe has been developed by PhD Kitty Steenberg to isotropically etch silicon left from
the KOH etching. The process parameters are stated in table 7.10. Due to the difference
in the amount of silicon left on the four wafers, see fig. 7.20, the etch times used differed.
The first wafer to be etched was wafer 04, which was etched for 70 seconds, and all the
silicon was observed to be gone afterwards. Wafer 10 was then etched for 3 minutes as
it had much more silicon on the surface. After the etch, it seemed like the silicon was
completely gone, but it seemed like there may have been some over-etching, and the
etch time was reduced for the next wafer was reduced. Wafer 02 was then etched for 2
minutes, and all the silicon was gone, meaning some over-etching had likely occurred. For
wafer 02, the void seen in fig. 7.18 was now a large bubble when it was transferred from
the process chamber to the load lock. A picture was not saved as the load lock was vented
immediately to prevent it from popping while in a vacuum, and it deflated when brought
back to ambient pressure. However, bubbles have been seen before, and an example
from a previously fabricated wafer in the MEMS applied sensors group can be seen in
appendix D. Wafer 08 was then etched for 2 minutes as well. However, after transferring
the wafer from the process chamber to the load lock, the entire top plate was gone, and all
processing had to be stopped on the wafer as there was no top plate left for the CMUTs.
Residues of poly-silicon and oxy-nitride had also been left in both the process chamber
and the load lock of the machine. Likewise, processing was also stopped in wafer 02

Wet Etching of Second BOX Layer
With the two remaining wafers, the last BOX layer had to be removed. To remove it, they
were etched in BHF for 7 minutes until all the oxide could be seen to have been removed.
With the oxide, the colour difference is a good indicator as it is blue/purple while the silicon
is grey. Also, the BHF has a low etch rate of poly-silicon, giving a good selectivity. [38]
During the etching, the wafers were also stirred to ensure an even etch rate across the
wafers and avoid the residues left where the wafers had rested in the wafer holder as with
the Cr earlier. After the BHF etch, the wafers were cleaned in piranha for 10 minutes to

remove any KOH etch traces from the wet etching of the handle silicon wafer. After the

any water left in the cavities that could not be removed with a nitrogen gun. All the handle
layers had now been removed from the two wafers, and only the top plate was left on the
borofloat wafer.
7.4.3
Opening Up To Bottom Electrodes
With the handle layers and other sacrificial layers removed, the top plate could be opened
selectively to access the bottom electrode for the wafers through the poly-silicon and the
oxy-nitride layers. To selectively etch through the layers, the wafers were pre-baked with
HMDS and then spin-coated with 2 µm of MiR positive tone photoresist. The photoresist
was then exposed using the same MLA system as before, where the third lithography
mask designed was aligned and exposed on each of the wafers with a dose of 320 mJ/cm2
and a defocus of 0. The wafers were given a PEB at 110 °C for 60 seconds and then
developed for 60 seconds in a puddle with TMAH to develop the exposed photoresist.
The alignment and the pattern were then checked using an optical microscope. By closing
the aperture of the light path as much as possible to increase the focus depth of the
microscope and shifting the focal plane below the top plate, the bottom electrodes could
be seen simultaneously as the developed photoresist. However, the trade-off is that both
the bottom electrodes and the photoresist will be in focus. While at the microscope, it
can be compensated by moving the focus up and down and focusing on the photoresist
and bottom electrodes in turns. An example from when the focal plane has been placed
between the photoresist and the bottom electrode can be seen in fig. 7.22 where the Au
below the poly-silicon top plate can be seen.
After the lithography and for etching down through the poly-silicon top plate, the wafers
had to be crystal bonded to a carrier again before the ICP system could be used to dry etch
the poly-silicon. The same ICP system was used, and the shallolr recipe was used again
for eight cycles. This process etched the poly-silicon without issues for both wafers. After
the etches, the wafers were quickly checked in an optical microscope to see the openings,
for both the underlying oxy-nitride could be seen. The wafers were then etched again in
the ICP system to etch away the oxy-nitride with the kitoxysi recipe. Wafer 04 was the
first to be etched. It was etched for 3 minutes and 20 seconds, the same as earlier.
After the etch, the photoresist could be seen to have been attacked by the oxygen from
the plasma, and some of the poly-silicon at the edge had been exposed to the plasma and
were darkened. Some electrode pads also showed damage from the plasma attacking the
Au, indicating the wafer had been over-etched. For wafer 10, the etch time was therefore
reduced to 1 minute. After the etching of wafer 10, the nitride was gone, and there was no
damage to the Au underneath. Just like the resist was also intact, camera photos of both
wafers can be seen in fig. 7.23. The extent of the damage to the electrode pads from wafer
04 can be seen in fig. 7.24. Despite the damage, it was decided to continue processing
the wafer as there still were arrays with minor damage, and the damage could have been
cosmetic. Still, it could be verified later with electrical characterisation. Another option

7.4.4
Top Electrode Deposition and Structuring
Following the plasma ashing, the wafer was taken directly for deposition of the Al for the
top electrode. Two newly bought borofloat wafers were included for the deposition and
used to time the etch rate of each design. In total, 1 µm of Al was deposited by e-beam
evaporation with a deposition rate of 10 Å/s. The deposition was done using the same e-
beam deposition system as previously. Following the deposition of the Al and to define the
etch mask for the top electrode. The wafers were baked with HMDS to improve adhesion.
This pre-bake was followed by a spin coating of 4.2 µm 5214E positive tone photoresist
on the four wafers, as the mask drawn is a negative mask and the drawn pattern was the
one which should not be etched.
Therefore, as earlier, the mask file was inverted digitally for the exposures using the same
MLA system. For each design, the dummy wafers were exposed first and then developed
to check the pattern. This time, two errors were caught this way. On the first dummy
wafer, a dose of 250 mJ/cm2 and a defocus of -2 was used. After the development of
the dummy wafers, it was found the defocus was too low, and the element labelling was
poorly defined. Therefore, the defocus was changed to 0 for the remaining exposures,
meaning a dose of 250 mJ/cm2 and a defocus 0 was used for all other exposures. For
the other dummy wafer, it was discovered that the design had not been inverted when
the exposure was performed. Therefore, the resist on that dummy wafer was removed by
plasma ashing it for 45 minutes with an oxygen flow of 400 mL/min, nitrogen flow of 70
mL/min and a power of 1000 W. The photoresist was then re-spin-coated and exposed
with an inverted mask. For wafers 04 and 10, the exposure was set up correctly with
74
Design, Fabrication and Characterization of a CMUT for a Linear Array Probe
==PAGE:83==
CHAPTER 7. FABRICATION
the mask file digitally inverted. The wafers were developed for 120 seconds with TMAH
in a puddle development and then rinsed with water to remove the remaining developer
using the same automatic developer as earlier. Following the lithography, the pattern was
quickly checked with an optical microscope, and an example from the electrode pads on
wafer 04 from array 1-2 and 7-2 can be seen in fig. 7.26. Unfortunately, the labelling of the
elements was not defined after the development, despite the change of defocus value.
This problem is most likely caused by a wrong dose or defocus value for the exposures.
However, the structures themselves were well-defined and had the correct dimensions.
The optimal dose and defocus value would need to be found by performing a dose and
defocus test with structures of similar dimensions on Al in the future. Since the design
itself had the correct dimensions and alignment. The fabrication was continued.

bottom electrode design, an etch time of 1 hour and 57 minutes was found, while for
the common top electrode design, an etch time of 1 hour and 55 minutes was found.
Therefore, wafer 10, common bottom electrode design, was etched first for 1 hour and
30 minutes, after which the Al as expected was not fully etched, and then for another two
times 10 minutes for a total etch time of 1 hour and 50 minutes. After this, the Al could
be seen to be fully etched. Wafer 04 was etched for 1 hour and 40 minutes. After the
etching, the pattern was defined, and the Alhad been removed from everywhere but the
top electrodes. An example of the etched kerf in between the top electrodes from wafer
04 can be seen in fig. 7.27

wafers were etched using the shallolr recipe again. However, the chuck temperature was
set to 0 °C to increase the etch selectivity. It also decreases the etch rate, so more etch
cycles are needed. Nine cycles were required to etch away all the poly-silicon to separate
the electrodes on wafer 10. A microscope image from wafer 10 can be seen in fig. 7.28

7.5
Chapter Summary
In this chapter, the fabrication of the PSOIs used, the bottom glass wafers and the pro-
cessing after the anodic bonding was described, along with the difficulties encountered
during the processing and how they were overcome to successfully fabricate an anodically
bonded wafer with a newly designed common bottom electrode 384-element linear array
and a Ti adhesion layer for the bottom electrode. The first such design to be fabricated
using the anodic bonding process in the MEMS applied sensors group.

This chapter will describe the electrical character-

wafer 10 with the common bottom electrode de-

These will have to be characterised in future work, as the results would still be relevant for
a new fabrication run and to understand further and validate the theory behind designing
CMUT arrays. An overview of the arrays and elements which were characterised is given
in fig. 8.1.
Before starting the characterisations, the theoretical value of the capacitance should be
calculated, as it can be used as a baseline for the measured capacitance and to de-
termine if something is wrong during the characterisations. The calculations were done
using eq. (2.6), as the capacitance is the zero bias capacitance where there should be
no deflection of the top plate. However, since there are two dielectric layers, the vacuum
and the oxy-nitride. The capacitance of an element will be that of two capacitor series,
giving the capacitance

For the area, the entire electrode area under the top electrode needs to be included,
meaning the connectors for the bottom electrode need to be included as well. For the
gap height and the nitride thickness, the measured values during the characterisations
were used for the most accurate estimate of the zero bias capacitance. By using these
values and eq. (8.1), a capacitance of 6.2 pF is obtained as the capacitance of the 65-
cell element and for a 5-cell element, the capacitance is 0.48 pF. Thus, values around
these capacitances are expected to be measured during the characterisations. The cal-
culations did not consider fringe effects. The first set of characterisations performed were
the impedance-based characterisations to determine if the resonance frequency of the
fabricated wafers matched that of the new FEM model and if the pull-in voltage was as
expected.

Design, Fabrication and Characterization of a CMUT for a Linear Array Probe
==PAGE:87==
CHAPTER 8. ELECTRICAL CHARACTERISATION
8.1
Impedance, Resonance, Pull-In and Stability
Measurements
For the impedance measurements, a OSC of 200 mV was used for all of the measure-
ments based on the results of the initial characterisations described in section 4.4. The
first impedance-based measurement performed was the determination of the unbiased
resonance frequency in air and at the operational bias, followed by the determination of
the pull-in voltage and, lastly, the stability of the elements during prolonged measure-
ments.
8.1.1
Resonance Frequency
The 65 cell test elements were used to determine the fabricated wafer’s resonance fre-
quency as they could be tested without damaging the 384-element linear arrays. The
first of the 65 cell test elements tested was the 65 cell test element 7-4, as it was in the
centre of the wafer and was not near any of the voids observed during the fabrication. A
DC bias from 0 to 100 V in 5 V increments was used to characterise the element to get
the unbiased resonance frequency. The frequency was swept from 1 to 40 MHz. While
the large frequency range is in case the resonance frequency appears higher than the
designed 28.1 MHz, see table 5.1, observing it would still be possible. When plotting the
measured phase angle and magnitude of the impedance as a function of the frequency,
no resonance peak could be seen when the applied DC bias was 0 V. However, the
phase angle was almost -90◦across the entire frequency range. The plot can be seen
in fig. 8.2a. When 5 V DC was applied to the element, a small resonance peak could
be seen at around 20 MHz in the measured phase angle. The plot is shown in fig. 8.2b
where it also can be seen the phase angle is still low at around -90◦. Therefore, based
on fig. 8.2, it would seem that the unbiased resonance frequency is much lower than the
designed unbiased resonance frequency. A Gaussian detrend performed by PhD Rune
Sixten Grass on the phase angle as a function of the frequency gave a central peak of
20.03 MHz. At 15 V DC, a Gaussian detrend showed a central resonance frequency of
20.02 MHz. Both of the Gaussian detrends can be seen in appendix G, giving the final
confirmation that the used FEM comsol model still needed more work for it to be able to
predict the experimentally determined resonance frequencies. However, with the fabri-
cated wafer and the impedance measurements obtained, the data obtained can be used
to further develop the models as there now is experimental data to compare FEM simula-
tions to where there are no doubts about the quality of the oxy-nitride due to the selectivity
of the top electrode etching. Due to time constraints, developing the model further during
this thesis was not possible and will have to be performed in future work.
The element’s resonance frequency moved towards lower frequencies when the DC bias
increased. The resonance peak for the phase angle also became more prominent. This
behaviour is in accordance with the appearance of a spring-softening effect from the in-
creased DC bias. A plot of phase angle from selected values of the DC bias can be seen
in fig. 8.3. Only a selection is shown as the plot otherwise would be too cluttered. A plot
with all the 21 values of the DC bias is shown in fig. E.1 in appendix E. When applying a
90 and 100 V DC bias, the phase angle sharply increases, and the resonance is now at
around 16 MHz and 14 MHz, respectively. The sharp increase and widening of the peak
would indicate that pull-in has started to occur earlier than the designed 125 V. However,
the peaks can be seen to be well-defined, with one central peak. The resonance peak at
pull-in should widen significantly more than in fig. 8.3b. This behaviour must be examined
more closely during a dedicated pull-in determination where smaller voltage increments
are combined with higher DC bias values to verify if it is the case for all elements or just
the single tested element. However, despite the sharp and well-defined resonance peak,
Design, Fabrication and Characterization of a CMUT for a Linear Array Probe
79
==PAGE:88==

the magnitude of the peak phase angle should be noted. It almost reached +60◦at 100
V. When compared to the phase angles measured at the designed operational DC bias
for the previously fabricated wafers in fig. 4.11, the phase angle only reached -78◦. Thus,
there is a significant increase in the response from the CMUTs in one element. With the
impedance measurement of the resonance frequency, it was decided to move on to deter-
mining the pull-in voltage. Further time could have been spent on a more rigorous study
of the resonance frequency. However, it was decided that the limited time would be better
spent determining the pull-in voltage.
8.1.2
Pull-in Determination
With the resonance frequency known and an indication that the pull-in voltage could be
lower than anticipated, the pull-in voltage now needed to be determined more accurately
by sweeping higher values of the DC bias in smaller increments for a more accurate
determination. The actual pull-in voltage was first attempted to be found using the 5 cell
test element elements at four different locations on the wafer. The exact locations can
be seen in fig. 8.1. These elements were initially used since it is not guaranteed the
insulating oxy-nitride will survive when a CMUT goes into pull-in, and the element could
be short-circuited as a consequence. For all elements, a DC bias of 80-130 V in 5 V
increments was used in the pull-in determination, except for one element where the bias
used was from -80 to -130 V in -5 V increments instead. The frequency was swept from
1-30 MHz, a reduced span than earlier now that the resonance was around 20 MHz. Out
of the four tested elements, three of them worked as a capacitor. However, a resonance
peak could not be seen for any of the three working elements, and the phase angle as a
function of the frequency can be seen in fig. 8.4. Here, the phase angle can be seen to
be the desired - 90◦for the three working elements, even when reversing the direction of
the electric field. Despite the correct phase angle, no resonance peak could be seen, and
pull-in could not be observed for the tested elements. The element that did not work could
have a phase angle that decreased linearly with the frequency and in the 0 to -20◦range.
80
Design, Fabrication and Characterization of a CMUT for a Linear Array Probe
==PAGE:89==
CHAPTER 8. ELECTRICAL CHARACTERISATION
(a) Phase angle for different of the DC biases.
(b) Phase angle for 100 V DC bias.
Figure 8.3: Impedance measurements from the 65 cell test element 7-4. On the left,
several different values of the DC bias are plotted, showing how the central peak moves
to lower frequencies when the bias is increased. On the right, the phase angle as a
function of frequency for a DC bias of 100V is shown.
For the three other elements, the absence of the resonance and absence of a change
in the phase angle for the different values of the DC bias would suggest the signal from
the 5 CMUT cells in the 5 cell test elements is too low for resonance to be measured.
From the theoretical calculation, the capacitance of one element should only be 0.48 pF.
Therefore, the pull-in voltage must be found from a full-length element with 65 cells as
they show a resonance peak and a downshift for the frequency where the resonance
peak occurs when increasing the applied bias.
It was decided to use the 65 cell test element 1-4 at the north of the wafer to perform the
pull-in determination as it was located far from the previously tested element but also next
to a 384-element linear array which looked like to be intact. For the pull-in measurement,
a DC bias from 80 to 140 V in 1 V increments was used so the pull-in could be determined
accurately. At the same time, the frequency was swept from 1-40 MHz. A selection of the
measurements in the range of 90 to 110 V with 2 V increments and a narrower selection
from 90-100V with 1V increments can be seen in fig. 8.5. The frequency for the peak
phase angle and the centroid phase angle are also plotted as a function of the applied
bias. From this figure, it would seem that the pull-in starts at around 106 V as the peak is
stationary with the frequency, and instead, the peak widens. Going to even higher values
for the DC bias, the peak widened even more but did not move much more in frequency.
The capacitance reported by the impedance analyser was also in the order of 16 pF or
more, indicating that the top plate had been completely pulled down to touch the bottom
electrode. Thus, the actual pull-in seems lower than the designed pull-in bias of 125 V,
even with the cavitation effects observed in section 7.3.4.
8.1.3
Stability
The last important parameter which can be obtained from the impedance measurements
is the stability of the elements. The previously characterised 65 cell test element 7-4 and
the 65 cell test element 7-3 were chosen to perform these characterisations. Different
voltage ranges were used to measure the stability of the elements. For the 65 cell test
element 7-4, the DC bias values were from 0 to 100 V in steps of 20 V as it had already
been subjected to 100 V when the resonance frequency was determined. While for the

65 cell test element 7-3, the stability was measured two times. The first time from 0 to
90 V in steps of 15 V and then the last step at 0 V. The second stability measurement
was performed from 0 to 100 V, then to -100 V, and lastly to 0 V in 20 V steps. Thus, the
elements were tested at the limits of where they were designed to operate. Both the 65
cell test element 7-3 and 7-3 were measured as 7-4 already may have gone into pull-in as
seen in fig. 8.3. For the 65 cell test element 7-4, the stability of the element was excellent.
The peak phase angle dropped from 54.9◦to 49.0◦over the 10 minutes at 100 V, and no
drop was seen at 80 V. Likewise, the capacitance dropped from 10.2 pF to 10.0 pF over
10 minutes at 100 V, and no drop was seen at 80 V. The phase angle and capacitance
plots can be seen in fig. 8.6. The phase angle can also be seen to have flattened out after
10 minutes, and the capacitance drop is also beginning to flatten out, meaning the values
are dropping to a fixed level. For the capacitance, the drop of 0.2 pF is a percentage-wise
drop of only 1.96 % over the entire 10 minutes.
For the 65 cell test element 7-3, a DC bias from 0 to 90 V in 15 V steps, followed by a 0 V
step at the end, was used for the first stability measurement. The lower maximal DC bias
was used to see how large the DC biases were and whether the elements were stable
before pull-in occurred. From the plots of the capacitance and phase angle in fig. 8.7,
the capacitance of the element dropped from 9.6 pF to 9.5 pF, a 1.0 % decrease over the
10 minutes. For the peak phase angle, the drop was from 8.5◦to 3.6◦. The plots also
show that the change decreases exponentially with time and almost flattened out after
10 minutes, meaning that the change from continuing the stability measurements would
have been minimal. Most important is the 0 V measurement at the end. Here, the peak
phase angle has dropped down to -90◦again, and the capacitance is at 8.6 pF, the same
value as the first measurements, proving the elements are fully stable up to 90 V.
With such an excellent stability measurement up to 90 V, a new stability measurement was
performed on the 65 cell test element 7-3 at a higher bias of 100 V in a measurement,

Figure 8.6: Plots of the peak phase angle and the capacitance from the stability measure-

which is comparable to the one used for the 65 cell test element 7-4. A negative bias was
used to reverse the field and see if there was any difference between the two directions
of the field. At the ± 100 V, a much more significant change could be seen over the 10
minutes the measurements were performed. The plot of the phase angle and capacitance
plot are shown in fig. 8.8. This time, the capacitance dropped from 10.8 to 10.2 pF, and the
peak phase angle dropped from 38.4◦to 32.9◦at a DC bias of 100V. Thus, the capacitance
dropped by 5.6 % over 10 minutes. While at -100 V, the capacitance dropped from 10.8 pF
to 9.0 pF, and the phase angle dropped from 45.6◦to 23.3◦. This change is a relative drop
in capacitance of 16.7 % over 10 minutes. Compared to the 65 cell test element 7-4, the
relative drop is significantly more significant at both 100 and -100 V. The large difference
between the measurements at 100 V and -100 V could also indicate that the elements
are more stable in the positive direction when the electric field points from the bottom
electrode to the top electrode. There is also -20 V where the phase angled suddenly
jumped to exactly 0.0◦, followed by it returning to -87.2◦again. There could be some
measurement error, or the element had taken some damage from the measurement when
using a negative bias. This behaviour could further indicate that stability is better when
the electric field is pointing up. However, further measurements would be required to
confirm this. These measurements were not performed, as it was instead decided to
spend the remaining time determining the yield and performance of the 384-element linear
arrays. Thus, this concluded the stability impedance measurements performed during
this thesis showed the newly designed common bottom electrode design had excellent
stability, despite the lower than designed pull-in voltage of the test arrays and resonance
frequency, which did not quite match the designed value.

into the performance of the CMUTs across the wafer, ideally as many of the 384-element

and the size is so minute compared to what has been seen earlier. [17] Still, it is the only

Based on the Zf measurement, 970 elements across all three arrays were working out
of 1152 elements characterised, meaning a yield of 84.2 % is obtained across the three
arrays. Most non-working arrays resulted from fabrication defects and were clear to see
with an optical microscope, such as the nitride defects, fig. 8.9a. Thus, they were easily
found from visual inspection of the arrays before performing characteristics. Common for
all functioning elements is that the measured capacitance is nearly 8 pF, almost 1.8 pF
higher than the expected value. The average capacitance across all working elements
was calculated to be 7.86 ± 0.25 pF. This high value and low capacitance spread can
also be seen from the bottom row in fig. 8.11. The large deviation between the mea-
sured and the estimated capacitance can be due to a combination of fringe effects and
the presence of parasitic capacitance somewhere in the circuit, which will increase the
measured capacitance of an element. An overview of the classification of the elements

8.2.1
CV curves
For the elements tested, CV curves were plotted for all of them to see the change in
capacitance as a function of the applied bias and to verify if the zero point of the CV curve
is at 0 V. Likewise, the capacitance should return to the same value afterwards as well.
A behaviour different from this could indicate charging as described in section 2.2.4. The
large DC bias used for the 384-element linear array 2-2 meant a hysteresis behaviour was
observed, especially for the negative bias. This behaviour was already indicated from the
stability measurements on the 65 cell test element 7-3 in fig. 8.8 as a possible issue when

was fitted to the CV measurements as the capacitance should scale with V 2, eq. (2.9).

accurate fit. An example from one of the working
elements from array 2-2 can be seen in fig. 8.12a.
From the CV curve, the increase in capacitance can
be seen to be significant. When +85 V DC is applied, the capacitance has increased from
7.56 to 17.73 pF, an increase of 134%. This large capacitance increase would indicate
that pull-in occurs at a lower bias than anticipated. At the same time, the CV curves seem
to indicate the elements can survive going into pull-in. This behaviour also explains why
the parabolic fit is inaccurate for the measurements, as it was never intended to describe
the capacitance near or at the pull-in voltage. It has its limit at 40 % of the pull-in voltage.
[6] From the CV curve in fig. 8.12a, it would also seem the element is capable of operat-
ing near the pull-in voltage and be able to still work afterwards as the capacitance returns
to the same value. Normally, at pull-in, the dielectric layers can break down, destroying
the element in the process. The pull-in voltage of the element is also lower than the one

The CV curve was more parabolic for the two other
characterised arrays, and the second-order polyno-
mial fit was closer to the experimental data. These
CV curves were measured at a lower DC bias to
avoid pull-in of the elements. A CV curve with a fit
is shown in fig. 8.12b for element 100 in array 7-2.
The fit is still imperfect as the C0 value is under-
estimated, and systematic errors exist between the
experimental data and the fit. However, going with
a pull-in voltage in the range of 85 V, as indicated
by the CV curve in fig. 8.12a, the 60 V used is 75
% of the pull-in voltage if the pull-in voltage is 80 V
and if the pull-in voltage is 110 V the 60 V is 54 % of
the pull-in voltage, placing the used voltage higher
than the limit of the model. Thus, this would explain
why the fit has systematic errors, as the model is
used over a range where it is no longer valid. De-
spite this, the increase in the capacitance is drastic. For element 100 in array 7-2 shown
in fig. 8.12b, the measured capacitance is 8.24 pF at 0 V and increases to 9.6 pF at ±
60 V. An increase of 16.5 % by just applying 60 V DC to the element, meaning that the
amount of electrical energy which can be stored in the capacitor is significant even at
small values for the DC bias. This large increase in the capacitance was observed for all
of the elements in arrays 6-2 and 7-2 declared as functioning by the Zf measurements.
8.2.2
Offset Voltage and Zero-Bias Capacitance
To determine the offset voltage and the zero-bias capacitance, the previously calculated
fits shown in fig. 8.12 could not be used as the presence of the systematic errors would
90
Design, Fabrication and Characterization of a CMUT for a Linear Array Probe
==PAGE:99==
CHAPTER 8. ELECTRICAL CHARACTERISATION
make the fitted values unreliable, and any conclusions drawn based on those fits would
also be susceptible to the same systematic errors. For example, the zero bias capaci-
tance was systematically underestimated as shown in fig. 8.12b. Therefore, the fit was
recalculated on a smaller part of the CV data to determine a reliable offset voltage and
zero-bias capacitance. The data was truncated for the fit, so only CV measurements per-
formed with a DC bias between -25 and 25 V were used. Truncating the data left 204
data points for each element on which the fit could be performed. Despite the refitting,
the fits for arrays 2-2 still had systematic errors from the hysteresis behaviour. On the
other hand, the fits for arrays 6-2 and 7-2 were almost perfect, and it was possible to fit
299 elements and 306 elements in arrays 6-2 and 7-2, respectively, with an R2 value of
0.99 or higher. Some elements were omitted as a hysteresis behaviour was observed,
which meant a good fit could not be obtained. Still, the amount of elements on which a fit
could be performed is high, and the criteria of an R2 > 0.99 is also a stringent criterion.
An example of a fit from 384-element linear array 7-2, element 100 is shown in fig. 8.13,
showing that the fit no longer has systematic errors when the fitting range is reduced. The
fitted values of the Voffset and C0 are summarised in two boxplots in fig. 8.14 showing the
fitted Voffset is close to 0 V for both arrays and tightly grouped. The close grouping of
the zero-bias capacitance means that the fabrication process gives uniform performance
across an array. While the offset voltage is small and tightly grouped, meaning that de-
spite the high DC bias used, the charging of the elements is negligible, further supporting
the results of the stability measurements described in section 8.1.3. For the fitted values,
a summary statistic is given in table 8.3 to illustrate further the high uniformity and low
value of the offset voltage

8.3
Chapter Summary
In this chapter, the electrical characterisation performed on the fabricated wafer was pre-
sented. The resonance was found to be lower than what was designed. Likewise, the
pull-in was found to be around 20-30 V lower, and results indicate it varies across the
wafer. The characterised elements were found to have excellent stability up to 90 V, and
the best stability was obtained when the electric field was oriented from the bottom elec-
trode up to the bottom electrode. On wafer scale characterisation, the yield was found to
be 84.2 % based on three fully characterised 384-element linear arrays where the capac-
itance was found to be larger than the theoretically calculated value, but with an increase
of nearly 25 % when biased at 60 V DC. The deviation from element to element was small
for all the working elements. Charging was also not observed as long as the elements

==PAGE:101==
9
Discussion
This chapter will discuss the difficulties encountered during the fabrication, which will need
further work to be fully understood and avoided in future work. The results of the electrical
characterisations will also be discussed. Both in terms of what was found for the fabricated
wafers, how they compare to previously fabricated wafers, and the characterisations that
should be performed in future work.
9.1
Fabrication Process in the Cleanroom
During the cleanroom fabrication, three major issues which could not be resolved during
this thesis were encountered. The first was the height of the Au after the removal of
the photoresist used as an etch mask for the bottom electrodes, the second was the
occurrence of voids for both of the wafers with Ta as the adhesion layer, and the third was
with the dry etching in the last process steps.
9.1.1
Height of Gold Bottom Electrodes
During the fabrication process, it was noticed that the final height of the Au electrodes were
less than anticipated after removing the photoresist used as an etch mask for the bottom
electrodes, see table 7.9. Notably, a considerable height variation was found across the
same wafers and from wafer to wafer. This significant height variation will lead to different
gap heights. It will influence the pull-in voltage, so different pull-in voltages are obtained
across a wafer and from wafer to wafer.
Four process steps could affect the height differences measured for the Au. The first is
from the deposition itself, the second is from the IBE process, the third is the wet etching
of the Au ribbons, and the fourth is from the resist stripping process. If the deposition
of the Au had resulted in the measured height differences, then the Au film would have
a thickness which was systematically off from the intended thickness as e-beam deposi-
tion is known for its high uniformity. Also, the Au deposition was done in four separate
batches so that any height difference would appear differently across the four batches.
As this was not observed, it is improbable to be the deposition which has resulted in the
height differences. The IBE process used to etch the electrodes is done on individual
wafers. However, the photoresist covered all of the Au electrodes after the etching, and
as described in section 2.5.2, the IBE process works by physically sputtering away the
etched material. Therefore, also ruling out the IBE process as the reason for the non-
uniformity. The third possibility of the wet Au etchant undercutting the photoresist and
etching the Au is also unlikely as the wafers were stirred. At the same time, the etching
and the pattern did not show significant differences across the wafers after the etching.
Therefore, if the Au etchant had been etched non-uniformly across the wafers, it would
also be seen in the pattern. The fourth option, photoresist stripping, is likely to have dam-
aged the Au, which has been seen before. [9] This process is known as cavitation and
occurs when the ultrasonic agitation used to loosen the photoresist also damages the Au
beneath it. Au is a soft metal that is much more susceptible to damage from ultrasonic
agitation. During this thesis, there was not time to explore alternatives or different pro-
cess parameters for removing the photoresist after the Au etching. Therefore, it would be
recommended that this be done in future work where the strength of ultrasonic agitation
should be investigated or if alternative processes can be used to avoid it altogether.
Design, Fabrication and Characterization of a CMUT for a Linear Array Probe
93
==PAGE:102==
CHAPTER 9. DISCUSSION
9.1.2
Gettering - Difference Between Ti and Ta
After the anodic bonding of the four wafers described in section 7.4.1, it was noticed that a
significant void plagued one wafer, and during later processing, the top plate fell off entirely
in a vacuum system for a different wafer. Both these wafers had Ta as the adhesion layer
beneath the Au electrode. The two other wafers with Ti as the adhesion layer did not
show the same problems. The voids encountered could be explained by the presence
of particles on the PSOI before the bonding. The appearance of voids has been seen
before on wafers where Ta was used as the adhesion layer. Therefore, it is interesting
that unexplainable voids and problems with the top plate sticking to the glass wafer were
observed only for the wafers with Ta, especially as the Ta has only been used once before
as an adhesion layer for anodically bonded wafers in the MEMS applied sensors group.
At that time, large voids were also noticed, but they now appear again in a new batch
of wafers, which have been processed as identically as possible to wafers where Ti was
used as an adhesion layer. A pattern is appearing. The sample size is still small, and a
dedicated study should be performed before any conclusion can be reached. However,
considering the theory presented in section 2.5.4, it is relevant to consider the possibility
that the material change affects the amount of residual gas inside the cavities following
the bonding process. The substrate used is known to outgas during the bonding process,
increasing the pressure inside the cavities and, in the end, can cause more voids. It is
possible that the previous use of Ti as the adhesion layer has acted as a getter, thus
trapping gas molecules, leading to a reduced pressure in the cavities as it is activated
at temperatures higher than 300 °C, which is below the 350 °C used during the anodic
bonding process. For Ta, the activation temperature as a getter is over 600 °C. Thus,
it will not be activated during the bonding process, and should not have any gettering
properties in the bonding process.
To see how much the 10 nm thin layer of Ti can getter. A calculation of how many gas
atoms there will be in the cavity of an element compared to the gettering properties of the
Ti can be used. The calculations are done for one cell to estimate the orders of magnitude.
One cell can be simplified as a cylinder with a radius of 21 µm and a height of 382 nm.
In these cylinders, there will be an inner cylinder with a radius of 19 µm and a height of
297 nm, which has to be subtracted from the total gas volume. This geometry gives the
following area:
Vgas = (Vcavity −Velectrode) = π

(21 µm)2 · 382 nm −(19 µm)2 · 297 nm

= 192.407 µm3
(9.1)
Using the ideal gas law and at standard pressure and temperature, the wafers were pre-
bonded at these conditions. The following number of atoms in the cavity is obtained:
Ncavity = P Vgas
RT
NA =
1 atm · 192.407 µm3
8.3145 J · mol−1 · K−1300 K · 6.022 · 1022mol−1 = 4.82 · 108 atoms
(9.2)
Thus, the amount of gas atoms in one cell is in the order of 108. For the number of Ti atoms
in the adhesion layer, the volume is also first found, and then the volume is converted into
the number of atoms in the solid.
VTi = π · (19 µm)2 · 10 nm = 11.3411 µm3.
(9.3)

==PAGE:103==
CHAPTER 9. DISCUSSION
Then, using the density of Ti of ρTi= 4.506 g/cm3, along with the molar mass of Ti which
is MTi=47.867 g/mol. The number of atoms can be found:
NTi = Vti · ρti ·

This calculation means the number of Ti atoms is larger than the number of gas atoms in
the cavity. Thus, it is possible that the Ti has acted as a getter and trapped the gas in the
cavity, reducing the pressure. It is not definitive proof that it is the absence of the gettering
properties, which meant both of the Ta wafers could not be finished. A study would have
to be done on several wafers, both with Ti, Ta and empty cavities with a similar volume
to study the effects of the bonding and if it was just a small sample size mixed with the
eventual randomness which follows from experimental lab work that meant it was the two
Ta wafers which had voids during the bonding process, especially as wafers with empty
cavities have been shown to bond before. Unfortunately, there was not time to set up
and perform such a study during this thesis. Still, examining the literature further and
performing such an experiment in future work would be beneficial and is recommended
to be performed.
9.1.3
Dry Etch Optimisations
Another part of the fabrication where further work could be performed is to open down
through the top plate to the bottom electrode. In this project, it was seen that the pho-
toresist used to shield the underlying poly-silicon was etched away when the oxy-nitride
was etched. This was due to the oxygen-rich plasma composition, which is incompatible
with the photoresist. Later in the processing, it was found that the places where the un-
derlying poly-silicon had been exposed to the plasma were difficult to etch away as if the
plasma had damaged the poly-silicon in some way, making it more challenging to remove
later at the last process steps. The issues with removing the poly-silicon can mean that
the top and bottom electrodes are not fully separated. The exact reason why the poly-
silicon is challenging to remove in these locations must be further examined, as it should
be avoided in future batches. The etch chemistry from the plasma may have somehow
affected the exposed poly-silicon. Therefore, plasma composition should be investigated
further. Another option could be to reduce the chuck temperature during the etching and
hopefully get a better selectivity between the photoresist and the oxy-nitride. Time con-
straints did not permit this to be examined during this thesis, and it would have to be done
in future work.
9.2
Characterisation
During the characterisation, the measured resonance frequency was significantly differ-
ent than expected from the simulations. It was expected to be 28.1 MHz in air while
unbiased. Instead, a resonance frequency of 20.02 MHz was measured with a DC bias
of 15 V, meaning a difference of around 8 MHz. Since the start of the fabrication, further
work on the COMSOL simulation model was performed by PhD Kitty Steenberg and PhD
Rune S. Grass, where changes were made to everything from the mesh used, the bound-
ary conditions defined in the model and the clamping distance used. With the new and
updated model simulations performed by PhD Kitty Steenberg and PhD Rune S. Grass
using the dimensions of the fabricated wafer, a theoretical resonance frequency is 21.2
MHz at 15 V DC bias, which is only 1.16 MHz, or a 5.7 % difference, more than the one
determined in section 8.1.1 showing that further work had been needed to develop the
Design, Fabrication and Characterization of a CMUT for a Linear Array Probe
95
==PAGE:104==
CHAPTER 9. DISCUSSION
models before the fabrication process was started to obtain a simulation model capa-
ble of predicting the experimentally measured resonance frequency. However, when the
fabrication was started, there was a lack of experimental data to compare it to. The unbi-
ased resonance frequency could have been determined for the two initially characterised
wafers, described in chapter 4, but it was feared the issues with the insulating oxy-nitride
and generally low amount of working elements meant that there were structural issues
which could affect the resonance frequency. Thus, time would have been spent optimis-
ing the model on elements with a fabrication issue that altered the resonance.
The new and updated model is still off with around 1 MHz from the experimentally mea-
sured value. The reason for this difference in resonance frequency is unknown, and
further investigation will be needed. Luckily, the wafer successfully fabricated during this
thesis has arrays with elements with different cavity radii, meaning the resonance fre-
quency can be measured as a function of the radius by characterising these elements
and then comparing the measured resonance frequency and the one simulated by the
COMSOL model. It will be possible to improve the model further. Therefore, despite the
model being much more accurate, plenty of work remains to optimise it to get even more
precise resonance frequencies for designs to be fabricated in the future.
9.2.1
Pull-In Voltage
The resonance frequency was not the only parameter found during the characterisations
that differed from the expected value found from the COMSOL model. According to the
COMSOL model, the pull-in voltage should have been 125 V. However, as found in sec-
tion 8.1.2 and section 8.2.1, the pull-in seemed to be lower than the designed 125 V.
Depending on where on the wafer, the characterisations were performed values from 80
V to somewhere in the range of 95-110 V were measured. The new version of the COM-
SOL model used to simulate the resonance frequency of the CMUTs also gives a pull-in
voltage of 114 V, a little lower than initially anticipated. In this simulation, the effects from
the cavitation effects found in section 7.3.4 were included, meaning if not for these ef-
fects, the pull-in voltage would have been even lower, which also explains why a pull-in
voltage of 85 V was measured at one spot. This measurement was at the north of the
wafer, where the lowest value of the gap height was found, see table 7.9, giving a possible
reason for the lower pull-in voltage.
Further characterisations would be needed to find the exact value of the pull-in voltage
and how it varies across a wafer, especially with the cavitation effects. The values of the
pull-in voltage can then also be used for further improving the simulation model, so the
pull-in voltage can be estimated accurately and to avoid having wafers, as those described
in chapter 4, where the pull-in voltage can not be determined experimentally because it is
too large. Unfortunately, the intended 5 cell test element could not be used for this as even
at large values of the applied DC bias pull-in was not observed. A resonance peak could
not be observed either, indicating that the elements were too short to give enough signal
for detecting the deflection of the top plate. Whether this is the case should be verified by
characterising more of the 5 cell test elements, both on wafer 10, which was fabricated,
but also on the previously fabricated wafers and by finishing the fabrication of wafer 04
with the common top electrode design to verify if the same behaviour is also present there.
There was not enough time to do these characterisations before the submission date of
the thesis, but they are strongly recommended to be performed in future work. If the
behaviour is observed again, then the length of the elements should be increased to get
a higher signal.
96
Design, Fabrication and Characterization of a CMUT for a Linear Array Probe
==PAGE:105==
CHAPTER 9. DISCUSSION
9.2.2
Stability and Direction of Electric Field
During the stability characterisations, section 8.1.3 and the full array characterisations,
section 8.2, it was found that the fabricated CMUTs seemed to be more stable when
the electric field was pointing up from the bottom electrode compared to when it was
pointing down towards the bottom electrode, meaning that the CMUTs seem to have a
preferred method for when they are being driven electrically. This behaviour is a bit curious
as the top plate oscillates due to the electrostatic force of attraction between the two
electrodes. Therefore, the direction of the field should not influence the stability of the
elements. However, for all measurements, a positive voltage is first applied to the bottom
electrode and then a negative voltage. Therefore, the initially measured direction could be
more stable, which explains the measured difference in stability. Further characterisations
were first a negative bias and then a positive bias is applied should be performed to
determine if there is a difference in stability or if previous measurements influence the
results. There was not time to perform these characterisations during this thesis, meaning
they should be performed in future work.
Despite this, the elements are stable with drops in the capacitance of 1.0 & over 10 min-
utes. The large-scale instability first showed up at 100 V during the impedance measure-
ments and with a pull-in voltage observed to be in the range of 80-110 V depending on
the array examined that places the voltage over the pull-in voltage to 90 % of the pull-in
voltage, further solidifying the stable behaviour of the elements. Indications were even
seen from one of the arrays that the CMUTs could go into pull-in and still be operated
afterwards without short-circuiting. Still, further stability measurements on the 65 cell test
element should be performed to verify the stability across wafers further, but the results
found during this thesis indicate excellent stability.
9.2.3
Yield and CV Measurements
For the full-sized 384-element linear arrays, many elements at the edges of the arrays had
issued with the oxy-nitride and, consequently, were not functioning. Luckily, the defects
could easily be seen with an optical microscope. Still, such systematic defects at the
same locations in different arrays should be investigated to see if they are present in all
of the arrays on the fabricated common bottom electrode wafer. The fabrication of the
common top electrode wafer 04 should also be finished so it can be characterised to see
if the same defects occur or if it is linked to the common bottom electrode design in some
way that is not yet understood.
Most of the other defects found could be explained by a bubble or other defects in the
photoresist etch mask used to separate the top electrodes, whether it was connected to
neighbouring elements or electrode pads being damaged. During the entire characteri-
sation, only one element was found where it was short-circuited and where it may be a
vertical short circuit between the top and bottom electrode that is the reason for it. Only
seeing one vertical short circuit is a significant improvement compared to earlier work,
where as many as 1/10 elements were short-circuited due to vertical shorts. [17] During
this thesis, a much lower voltage of 150 V was used compared to the previously used 600
V during the anodic bonding. The lower voltage had been shown in earlier work to reduce
the amount of vertical shorts between the top and bottom electrodes, which is not a new
result. Still, it further proves that lowering the bonding voltage leads to a lower amount of
vertical short circuits and shows that the elements fabricated are stable and perform well
during the electrical characterisations. Especially when the change in capacitance is so
drastic as was measured, and a relative change of over 20 % was observed for the work-
ing elements at ± 60 V. Such an excellent change in capacitance will hopefully lead to
an increased acoustic performance of the arrays and will have to be determined in future
Design, Fabrication and Characterization of a CMUT for a Linear Array Probe
97
==PAGE:106==
CHAPTER 9. DISCUSSION
work by mounting a functioning and characterised array to a PCB and encapsulating it in
PDMS. It would also be beneficial if one of the variable cavity radii test arrays is mounted
in a PCB and encapsulated in PDMS as the COMSOL model then can be even further
improved to find the resonance frequency of encapsulated arrays, and hopefully making it
possible to hit the targeted immersion resonance for arrays to be designed in future work.
9.3
Comparison to Previous Work
On previously fabricated anodic bonded wafers, the change of capacitance at a DC bi-
asses of 100 V has been on the order of 0.2 pF, going from around 7.8 to 8.0 pF. [18]
Limiting the DC bias to 60 V DC, the change was 0.1 pF. [18] For wafers fabricated with
a LOCOS process and elements with the same amount of CMUT cells. With dimensions
comparable to an anodically bonded array, a capacitance change of 0.1 pF, going from
4.63 to 4.73, has been found at a DC bias of 100 V. [15, 14] The designed, fabricated
and characterised arrays in this work were found to have a change in the capacitance of
almost 2 pF when just 60 V DC was applied to them. This difference gives an increase
of the capacitance of nearly 25 % when 60 V DC is applied, placing them significantly
higher than the previously fabricated wafers in the MEMS-applied sensors group. The
wafer fabricated in this thesis had a lower pull-in voltage, which could be why. It was
also possible to observe pull-in of the few tested elements. For the previously fabricated
fusion bonded wafers, pull-in could not be determined experimentally. [15, 14] The same
was found for the anodically bonded wafers fabricated in earlier work. [18] Where char-
acterisations performed in this thesis could not determine the pull-in voltage as described
in chapter 4. The yield of the wafer fabricated during this thesis is also higher than the
previously fabricated anodically bonded linear arrays, marking another improvement.[18]
Still, the fusion bonded LOCOS wafers have a better yield at over 99 %. [15] Meaning
there is still space for further improvement of the fabrication. Still, the defects are easier
to see and classify and are now in the form of connected top electrodes, visible nitride
defects, or errors with the resist used as an etch mask, see fig. 8.9, instead of the vertical
short which were difficult to identify and need to be found by examining the back side of
the wafers. [17]
Overall, the work done in this thesis has shown improvements to the fabrication of the
linear anodically bonded process and fabricated arrays where such a significant relative
change in the capacitance has not been seen before for both the LOCOS based process
and the anodic bonding process.
9.4
Chapter Summary
This chapter discussed the problem of gettering, which may have been the reason for the
large voids observed on the anodically bonded wafers where Ta was used as the adhesion
layer. The cavitation problem was also discussed, and further study was suggested to
eliminate it in future wafer batches to obtain more uniform pull-in voltages across a wafer.
The issues with the dry etching of the oxy-nitride and the damage to the photoresist etch
mask were discussed, and two ways to eliminate it were suggested. Lastly, the results
of the electrical characterisations were discussed, as well as what future work should
be performed and how the results from the fabricated wafer land compare to previously
fabricated wafers.
98
Design, Fabrication and Characterization of a CMUT for a Linear Array Probe
==PAGE:107==
10
Conclusion & Outlook
This thesis aimed to design, fabricate and characterise a CMUT for a linear array probe.
To achieve this goal, an initial electrical characterisation was performed to determine the
optimal design parameters and fabrication method. It was determined that the pull-in
voltage should not be larger than 125 V to avoid charging effects during the operation of
the CMUTs, and the top electrodes should be etched using an Ion Beam Etching (IBE)
process to avoid damaging the insulating oxy-nitride.
Based on this, a 384-element linear array with a resonance frequency of 15 MHz in im-
mersion was designed with a λ/2 pitch of 51.33 µm for a linear array probe. Two Finite
Element Method (FEM) models were initially used and compared to design the CMUT
array. It was found that one used a different material selection. Therefore, work was
focused on a FEM model designed for anodically bonded structures. In the model, the
inclusion of a PDMS layer on top of the CMUT cell was added to the model to simulate
the frequency when the CMUT array is cast into the probe. From the simulations, it was
found the circular CMUT cells should have a radius of 21 µm, a bottom electrode radius
of 19 µm, a gap height of 85 nm and a top plate thickness of 4 µm poly-silicon. It should
have a 28.1 MHz unbiased resonance frequency in air.
Two new test arrays for determining the effective Young’s modulus of the top plate and
how the pull-in voltage depends on electrode radius were designed and included in the
lithography mask sets for further improving the simulation models used after the fabrica-
tion.
To compare the fabricated design to previously fabricated anodically bonded and LO-
Cal Oxidation of Silicon (LOCOS) based designs. Two sets of lithography masks were
made with the same CMUT dimensions but with different bottom and top electrode con-
figurations, combined with titanium and tantalum as the adhesion layer for the bottom
electrodes. The fabrication of four different combinations spread out over 12 wafers was
started, of which only one wafer was finished during this thesis due to difficulties encoun-
tered in the clean room. The successfully fabricated wafer was a common bottom elec-
trode design with a titanium adhesion layer for the bottom electrode. It is suspected that
there currently is an issue with using tantalum as the adhesion layer. The exact reason
is unknown; further work will be needed to determine it.
The fabricated wafer with a common bottom electrode and titanium adhesion layer was
characterised. The unbiased resonance frequency was found to be 20.03 MHz in air, the
biased resonance frequency in air was determined to be 14-16MHz, and the pull-in voltage
was determined to be in the range of 80-110 V and seemed to vary across the wafer.
Therefore, further work is still needed to optimise the models to get the desired resonance
frequencies and pull-in voltages. Across three of the characterised 384-element linear
arrays, a yield of 84.2 % was found, and the majority of the non-working elements were
due to nitride damages at the edges of the array or bubbles in the photoresist. A 20-25 %
change in the capacitance was found for the working elements when 60 V DC was applied,
which is more than previously seen for both a LOCOS and anodic bonding process. At
the same time, the fabricated CMUTs showed excellent long-term stability. Further work is
still needed to characterise more of the 384-element linear arrays, along with more pull-in
and stability measurements and characterise the two new test arrays to further improve
the FEM models used to simulate and design the CMUTs. Likewise, there was not enough
Design, Fabrication and Characterization of a CMUT for a Linear Array Probe
99
==PAGE:108==
CHAPTER 10. CONCLUSION & OUTLOOK
time to mount an array in a probe and characterise it acoustically. Therefore, this should
also be done in future work. The wafer with the common top electrode design should be
finished to fully compare the LOCOS and anodic bonding processes.

Glossary
16 element 340 µm pitch test array Test array with the same size as the 16 elements
filled array but with only 16 linear elements placed with a pitch of 340 µm. 23–29,
31, 44–46
16 elements filled array Large 2D array made up of 112 elements with a λ/2 pitch. How-
ever, they are divided into 16 groups of 7 elements. 23, 44–46, 107
384-element linear array The full 384-element array designed in this thesis with a λ/2
pitch. It has 384 linear elements, each with 65 individual CMUT cells. iii, 23–26, 28,
42–46, 67, 71, 72, 77–79, 81, 82, 84, 85, 88–91, 97, 99, 107
5 cell test element Small test structure to determine pull-in voltage locally on the wafer
near the 384-element linear array. Are placed along the bottom electrode pad for
the 384-element linear array. 46, 47, 80–82, 96
65 cell test element Test structure with 65 CMUT cells. They are located between the
larger elements and allow for more local tests of pull-in voltage and resonance fre-
quency on full-length elements. 24, 45, 47, 79–81, 83–86, 88, 97, 141, 146
AFM Atomic Force Microscopy - A characterisation method for measuring the surface
profile on nanometer scale and all the way down to picometer scale. Ideal charac-
terisation method for finding the nanoscale roughness of a wafer surface. 4, 51, 52,
55
NMP Remover 1165 Solvent used for stripping photoresists after having used them as
etch masks. Typically combined with ultrasonic agitation swell to reduce the time
needed before the photoresist is dissolved.. 59, 63
oxy-nitride silicon nitride, which has been oxidised to give an oxygen-rich silicon nitride
film.. 31, 48, 52, 54–56, 66–68, 71–73, 78–80, 86, 95–99
PES Etch Mixture PES 77-19-04 Selectipur. Etchant used to wet etch aluminum in DTU
nanolab’s cleanroom. It is known to be able to etch isolating nitride. [38]. 23–25
piranha Mixture of 98 % sulfuric acid and 30 % hydrogen peroxide mixed in the ratio
of 4:1 and sometimes in 3:1 ratio for more aggressive etch to clean the surface of
wafers from organic traces and alkali ions. 51, 55, 59, 60, 63, 64, 69, 71
pitch The pitch is the distance between the same spot on two different objects. For
example, in the case of two parallel lines. The pitch of the two lines will be the
distance between the two centres of the lines. 19, 32, 42, 43
pull-in The behaviour of a CMUT when the forces from the applied bias exceed those of
the spring force in the top plate. Pulling the top plate down so it touches the bottom
electrode. iii, 7, 9, 10, 13, 14, 17, 20, 21, 23, 24, 26, 27, 30, 32–36, 38, 39, 45–47,
63, 78–81, 83–85, 89–93, 96–99
Design, Fabrication and Characterization of a CMUT for a Linear Array Probe
107
==PAGE:116==
Glossary
RCA Standard cleaning process used in the cleanroom consisting of a series of chemical
baths, RCA 1 and RCA 2, with HF dips in following each of them. Is a requirement
before putting wafers in the furnaces inside the cleanroom. 49, 50, 52, 56, 64
RCA 1 The first chemical bath used in the RCA cleaning process.
It is a mixture of
NH4OH, H2O2 and H2O (Di-water) mixed in the volume ratio of 1:1:5 and is heated
to 70 °C. 49, 53, 54, 64, 108
RCA 2 The second chemical bath used in the RCA cleaning process. It is a mixture of
HCL, H2O2 and H2O (Di-water) mixed in the volume ratio of 1:1:5 and is heated to
70 °C. 49, 108
variable electrode radii array Test array with 9 different bottom electrode radii. Can be
used to determine the pull-in voltage dependence on the electrode radius experi-
mentally. 45, 46, 78
variable radii test array Test array with 9 different cavity radii. Can be used to determine
the Youngs modulus of the top plate experimentally. 44–46, 78
108

Crystal bond
Hotplate
90-
110C
Dummy wafer is put facing up on tissue paper on
hotplate.
Crystal bond is applied from the cen-

placed on top with glass-side facing down. Stack is
removed from hotplate and cooled down.
Recipe: Standby, Pins down

crystal

evenly
and
do not apply too
much,
so that it
spills out.

check
that wafer stack is
bonded.

colour
change
is
seen
when etch is done.
Should
resemble
Si.

of for 30 seconds in

(A3)

LPCVD
Poly-
Si (4”) (B4)
Place a test wafer in the center of the boat and place
device wafers equally distributed on each side. No
unfilled slots between wafers. Fill up with dummy
wafers at the outer spaces.
always use slow open.
As target thickness may vary, use the latest depo-
sition rate from LabManager for the initial deposi-
tions. Check thickness between depositions.
Final thickness: 4.1 µm
Recipe: POLYBOR
Dep1:
≈1.68 µm,
Dep2:
≈1.24 µm,
Remove
wafers for thin PSOIs, Dep3: ≈1.24 µm

large layers. So 3 hr & 00 min for 1.68µm & 2hr &
18 min for 1.24 µm
It is not allowed
to deposit for more
than 3 consecutive
hours, divide depo-
sition into shorter
intervals.

hand, keep everything wet, and to perform brush

(though > 20 µm), the shims may not match their
stated thickness. Place the wafer in water storage
after polishing.

of max.
